EXTRA SESSION
OF CONGRESS
ON CURRENCY
Sentiment in Favor of
Prompt Action.
VIEWS OF LEADING MEN
REPUBLICANS SHOULD REDEEM
THEIR PLEDGES.
The Best Interests of the Nation
Demand Legislation to Reform
the Money Sys
tem.
The eentlment of California as ex
pressed by leading public men and intelli
gent bankers who have Btudled the money
question is positively in favor of na
tional legislation to bring about currency
reform. The judgment of the majority
of business men of large affairs is that
an extra session of Congress should be
called by the President. Argument is ad
vanced that the important questions relat
ing to currency reform should be consid
ered apart from other issues, and not be
thrown into the general discussion of
topics springing from the war. An arti
cle published in The Call of Tuesday last
engaged the attention of the commercial
community and elicited many expressions
of opinion on the subject.
Committed to
Currency Reform
Ex-Senator C. N. Felton Holds
an Extra Session Should
Be Called.
Ex-Senator Charles I*. Felton is out
spoken in favor of an extra session of
Congress to consider the currency reform
question. He says: :.-â ;,:â¢.-; i
"The Republican. party, realizing that a
reform of the currency is essential to the
best interests of the nation, is committed
to action on the, question. In my opinion
the sooner the action Is taken the better,
and that by all means in a session of
Congress called for the purpose. In no
other way canit receive the undivided at
tention requisite for. a wise conclusion."
â¢ â 
An Extra Session
by All Means
Congressman-Elect Julius Kahn
Strongly Favors the
Proposition.
Congressman-elect Julius Kahnâ l am
in thorough accord with the idea pro
mulgated in The Call's editorial of Tues
day. I have always believed It necessary
to hold an extra session of Congress to
settle the currency question and give the
legislation upon it a good, fair chance to
operate before the national election of
1900. The great trouble with this country
is that it does not give time for a settled
policy to become fixed before Congress
meets again and changes of such magni
tude are made that the original legisla
tion is nullified. If an extra session be
called by the President, and I trust that
it will, the currency question would prob
ably be settled inside of three months,
and then there would be over a year be
fore the next ensuing elections; within
which time the new laws would have had
a chance to operate and the administra
tion would be in a position to know just
what defects exist and how to remedy
them. Whereas if legislation is delayed
until the regular session, before it is fairly
tested the elections of 1900 will be held.
The newly elected Congress would then
begin to tinker with said legislation so
that the entire effect of it would be lost
to the nation.
What the Interests of tho country de
mand at the present time is stability.
The constant tinkering with tariff arid
financial legislation causes our merchants
and manufacturers to refrain from mak
ing any large investments, and conse
quenty the laboring mail, the mechanic
and, in fact, all our people suffer more or
less. By all means let us have an extra
session to settle the momentous question
of currency reform.
Stable Currency
Begets Prosperity
Extra Session Meets With the
Entire Approval of Banker
I. W. Heliman.
President I. TV. Heliman of the Ne
vada Bankâ l am of the firm opinion that
the President should call an extra session
after next March to settle for all time
the most important question of the cur
rency. We want stable money, for no
country can have permanent prosperity
without It. Every civilized nation on
the face of the globe is to-day on a gold
basis. Semi-civilized countries like China,
South America and Central America are
the only ones left on a silver basis.
Wherever that currency exists labor is
degraded and the people are in a poor
condition generally.
Our soldiers in Manila, Cuba and Porto
Rico have had a splendid object lesson on
the benefit of sound money and the need
of proper financial legislation. Every dol
lar of our money brought them *2 20, and
when they return to us they will not be
in favor of a depreciated currency, be
cause they have realized the benefit of
our money.
By an extra session of Congress legisla
tion can be enacted for the United
States to remain permanently on a gold
standard, and that menace of the free and
unlimited coinage of silver can be forever
silenced and destroyed. This subject Is of
moment not only to the bankers but to
the whole business community as well. A
stable currency begets prosperity, and
the question is of sufficient magnitude to
warrant the calling of an extra session for
its consideration.
a
Currency Reform
Is Imperative
Warren Olney Says If an Extra
Session Will Bring It About,
Let Us Have It.
"Warren Olneyâ l heartily agTee with the
proposition of legislating on the subject
of currency reform, but I am not pre
pared at the present time to state that
the conditions are of such a nature as to
warrant the calling of an extra session
for so doing. I lean somewhat to the
opinion that postponing the considera
tion of the question may bring about more
by natural growth than if special action
were taken upon it. The trend is such
that currency reform Is coming very soon
by the inevitable progress of events,
whether a special session be held or not.
There is danger in extra sessions ot going
into matters that ought to be left alone.
I am not an adherent of over legislation,
and extra sessions have not been bene
ficial in the past. I am inclined to think
: that better results can be obtained at the
regular session of Congress in December.
I don't know, however, but that the ad
vantages of an extra session would out
weigh what we old veterans are inclined
to anticipate from such a course. There
Is no doubt that attention would be cen
tered on the subject if an extra session
is held. It would be a wonderfully great
educating influence, and possibly we
would after all obtain tÃe Dest legisla
tion that way. Good currency legislation
will restore confidence, -and if an extra
session -will carry out those conditions
then let us have It, for we will thus re
alize a direct benefit.
I am satisfied that the silver craze is
rapidly dying out and will never again be
a vital issue before the country. The
party that goes against the natural laws
of finance, if I may use that expression,
will be ground to powder. Let this Gov
ernment follow the example of the lead
ing nations of Europe and provide fqr a
stable currency, but not by the issuance
of Government bills, as at the present
time. Let legislation be of such a char
acter as to throw such safeguards around
our currency as to render it invincible.
Should Be Settled
Without Delay
Ex-Mayor Pond Holds That the
Party Pledge Should Be
Fulfilled.
Ex-Mayor E. B. Pond entertains positive
opinions on the subject of currency re
form. He says: "The currency system
ought to be settled without delay. The
promise made by the Republicans to the
people of the country should be redeemed.
In my judgment, an extra session of Con
gress, if necessary, should be called to
legislate upon the question. The adjust
ment should be madÂ© before the next
Presidential campaign. The gold Demo
crats expect a prompt solution of the
monetary question. They voted for Presi
dent McKinley on this ground, and they
have the right to urge that the promise
made to the voters shall be redeemed.
The Republicans, having a majority in
Congress, are responsible. The sound
money men expect that measures will be
taken without unnecessary delay to re
form the currency."
Foreign Policy of
the Government
Banker P. N. Lilienthal Is Not
Able to Judge Whether An
Extra Session Is Demanded.
P. N. Lilienthal, manager of the Anglo-
California Bank, believes that a perma
nent settlement of the monetary policy
of the country is desirable. He says:
"The currency question ought to bo set
tled, but whether an extra session of Con
gress ought to be called for that purpose
is another proposition. I am not able to
judge whether an extra session will be
required. Again, it must be taken into
account that man$ r questions of vital im
portance growing out of territorial ex
pansion may arise. Legislation in this
regard may assume so much importance
that an extra session bearing on the so
called foreign policy of the United States
will be demanded. I am not competent,
therefore, to judge what the interests of
the country will require. The currency
question, I admit, ought to be settled; but
whether it should be disposed of at an
extra session as the tariff question was
is another matter."
Settlement in
Regular Session
Thomas Brown Fancies an Ex-
tra Session Is Not
Needed.
Thomas Brown, cashier of the Bank of
California, says: "The question of cur
rency reform ought to be settled, but it
is not of sufficient importance to justify

An Advocate â¢ .
of Sound Money
Manager Grecnebaum o? the Lon-
don and Paris Bank Favors
Immediate Action.
Sigmund Greenebaum, manager of the
London, Paris and American Bank, is an
advocate of sound money and the immedi
ate adoption by Congress of a sound
money policy. He says: "The set
tlement of the currency question in
a proper manner will be beneficial to

Is only one way to settle the monetary

A Fair Degree
o? Prosperity
Banker James K. Wilson Doubts
the Advisability of Agitating
the Currency Question.
James K. Wilson, president of the San
Francisco National Bank, holds that the
promise of a sound currency should be
fulfilled, but doubts whether the inter
ests of the country would be benefited by
un<iue agitation of the question. He Bays:
"We are enjoying a fair degree of pros
perity at present, with indications of a
larger measure or prosperity. Agttation
of the currency question, such agitation
as an extra session of Congress on the
subject would cause, might alarm the
holders of money. When monetary legis
lation is discussed money hides away"!
Unless the holder of money is a broad
minded man of affairs he is apt to Keep
his cash idle, pending the settlement of
monetary issues. In my judgment a cur
rency reform measure in accordance .with
the pledges, made in the platform of the
successful party should be passed by
Congress, but I am not prepared to say
that an extra session should be called."
An Extra Session
Should Be Called
Governor Budd Holds That the
Currency Should Be Con
sidered Separately.
Governor Budd maintains that Congress
should settle the money question. He
says: "An extra session ought to be
called to consider currency reform. In
the 'hurly-burly' of a regular session,
pending, the monetary policy of the Gov
ernment cannot be properly considered.
Now the tir.ie of the regular session will
be taken up in the consideration of ques
tions growing out of the war. The dis
cussions will be animated, earnest and
perhaps bitter, by reason of wide diver
gence of opinion. In my judgment the
subject of currency reform should not be
thrown into the discussion when the
minds of members are engrossed in con
sidering the settlement of war issues. It
is desirable that stability should be given
to the currency of the country. An extra
session should be called to consider the
question of reform in our currency, and
the adjustment should be permanent as
far as possible under our institutions of
government. The Republicans pledged
in their platfrom that they would bring
about the desired reform, and by so pledg
ing gained the support of many gold Dem
ocrats. It is now the duty of the Repub
lican party to redeem the pledge."
Question of Vital
Importance
Victor Metcalf, Congressman-
Elect, Favors Prompt Settle-
ment of Vexatious Problems.
Victor Metcalf, member of Congress
elect from the Third District, advocates
prompt action. He says:
"I consider the money question one of
vital importance to the people of this na
tion. If not disposed of at this session
of Congress I believe that the best in
terests of this country would be sub
served by calling an extra session for
the purpose of permanently settling this
vexatious problem."
A Great Bussian Coming.
Customs Collector Jackson was notified
yesterday by the Department of State
that Grand Duke Cyril, a cousin of the
Czar of Russia, would arrive at San Fran
cisco on the steamer China on the 27th
of this month. The Collector was in
structed to receive the Grand Duke with
all the honors due his station, to pass his
baggage and that of his suite without ex
amination and to take his word as to the
character and quantity of dutiable goods
contained therein.

HEARST'S PLAN CONSIDERED
GALLAGHER AND FAY IN A
"i; WORDY SET-TO.
Some Small Embarrassment.: Caused
by the \Jnexpected Absence
of a : Few of , the \ "
â¢ â¢./Members, -â¢
A meeting: of the Democratic State Cen
tral Committee was held at the California
Hotel yesterday afternoon, pursuant to
a call Issued about two weeks ago. It
was generally understood that the pur
pose of the meeting was the discussion of
a plan of reorganization of the party in
the city and State In accordance with a
proposal made by W. R. Hearst, pro
prietor of the Examiner.
Those present at yesterday's meeting
were J. L. Gallagher and Charles W. Fay.
In the absence of Chairman Mann, Mr.
Gallagher called Mr. Fay to order. The
secretary of the committee being absent.
Mr. Fay assumed the duties of the office,
and having called the roll found that Mr.
Gallagher was present. A slight delay
was occasioned by the non-arrival of
Treasurer Bart Burke, who has a great
head for figures and who knows the exact
amount of the deficiency that Mr. Hearst
is to make good in case he is allowed 10
reorganize the party on "not only raised
but nailed" lines. As soon as it became
apparent that Mr. Burke was at his home
in Santa Cruz Mr. Gallagher appointed
Mr. Fay to act as treasurer. Mr. Fay's
first official act was to fine Mr. Gallagher
25 cents, the same to be expended on
refreshments for the crowd. Mr. Gal
lagher, as chairman, remitted the fine,
but Mr. Fay appealed from the decision of
the chair and succeeded in making good
his contention by a vote of 1 to 0.
Mr. Fay created something of a Btir
in the meeting by introducing a motion
to the effect that no smoking be allowed
during the proceedings. A vote was
taken, which resulted in a tie, there being
1 aye and 1 no. A long and acrimonious
debate followed, which ended In a com
promise being effected by Mr. Gallagher
agreeing to allow Mr. Fay to smoke the
latter half of the cigar that started the
controversy.
A stir of expectancy swept over the as
semblage when Mr. Gallagher called Mr.
Fay to the chair and took the floor.
Mr. Fay rapped for order, and by way of
a precautionary measure stated that no
disturbance of any kind would be per
mitted. He then appointed Mr. Gallagher
to act as sergeant-at-arms and. instructed
him to preserve order. This course had
the desired effect, and as soon as order
had been restored Mr. Gallagher asked
permission to address the meeting. When
it was granted he said:
"Mr. Chairman, I move we adjourn
When the ripple of excitement that fol
lowed tnis speech had subsided, Mr. Fay
in "Do e ii d hear a second to the gentleman's
m There being no second to the motion it
was declare!! out of order. Mr GaJs-
Kher resumed the chair and Mr Fay
Claimed the floor on a question of per
sonal privilege. He referred to the motion
he had previously made regarding the
right of members to smoke during the
progress of the meeting. He then called
attention to the fact that the time had
arrived when his rights under the adjust
ment that had been made should be ac
corded him. , .Jr.' yJ
Mr Gallagher suggested that the mat
ter of the member's rights in the prem
ises be submitted to an arbitration com
mittee of two. of Which the chairman
should be a member. This was agreed to
and Mr. Gallagher appointed Mr. Fay as
the other member of the committee. A
recess of five minutes was then taken in
order to give the commute time in which
to meet and prepare Us report.
Mr Gallagher called the arbitration
committee to order. He then called the
attention of his fellow member to the fact
that there was very little left to arbitrate,
and that unless Mr. Fay had a cigar
holder an award in his favor would be
of small value- There was no combating
the logic of this argument and Mr. Fay,
wno is trying to stop smoking anyway,
consented to drop the' matter until the
question of the reorganization of the
party had been finally determined.
When the Central Committee reassem
bled Mr. Fay called the roll and found
Mr. Gallagher still present. Mr. Gal
lagher stated that in the event of two
members claiming the floor at the same
time he would decide in favor of the one
nearest the seat provided for the use of
the secretary-
Mr. Fay caught the eye of the chair
man,'and upon being recognized ex
plained that on account of the unavoid
able absence of Mr. Tarpey and others he
wns in favor of postponing the matter of
the reorganization of the. party until after
Judge Maguire has finished his self-ap
pointed task of reorganizing Father
Yorke. This seemed to meet with general
approval. Mr. Fay then moved that the
hall rent be charged to the deficiency that
Is to be assumed by Mr. Hearst. There
being no objection it was so ordered. Mr.
Gallagher then declared the meeting ad

CUSTOM HOUSE DRAYING.
Manager Fuller Denies the Statement
Made by a Pair of Discon
tented Brokers.
"W. H. Fuller, manager of the Over
land Transit Company which does the
draylng for the Custom-house, is wroth,
over the statement of two brokers to the
effect that his company la not making
Its deliveries expeditlously. He would
like to ascertain the identity of the brok
ers for the purpose of convincing them
that they are mistaken. Mr. Fuller says
the delay in delivery of the cargo on
the Shangtung was caused by the break
ing down of the boilers in the basement
of the Appraisers' building, thus stop
ping the freight elevators for ten days.
He asserts that whenever the permits are
issued his drays promptly deliver the
goods, and that the complaints of the
two brokers are blasts of wind through
holes in their hats.
DENNERY OPENS HIS FIGHT.
Contests the Election of R. Porter
Ashe, State Senator-Elect.
Leon Dennery, confident that he was
counted out at the recent election, con
tests the right of R. Porter Ashe. who has
been declared elected to represent the
Twenty-fourth Senatorial District in the
next Legislature. Dennery filed his
grounds of contest with the County Clerk
yesterday, and the matter has been sub
mitted to Justices of the Peace Kerrigan
and Groezinger.
Dennery avers that Ashe was not le
gally nominated by the Democratic party
for the office of State Senator and his
name was printed as a candidate without
authority of law; that Ashe was not a
resident of the Twenty-fourth District for
one year next preceding his election; that
his certificate of nomination was not
properly filed; that Ashe and his friends
expended money far in excess of the
amount allowed by law; that contrary to
law the voting booths were opened be
fore sunrise and closed long after sunset;
that votes for Dennery were counted for
Ashe. and, in fact, the defeated candidate
â sets forth every conceivable ground that,
if substantiated, might turn defeat to vie-
Dennery was laeaten by but twenty
five votes, and he is of the opinion that In
the' investigation to come he will win out
STRUCK BY A TRAIN.
B. L. McDonald, a Laborer, Seriously
Injured on Channel Street.
B. L v McDonald, a laborer, was struck
by the return train from Ingleside late
yesterday afternoon and seriously in
jured. He was removed to the Receiv
ing Hospital, where Dr. Rinne found that
he had sustained a compound fracture of
the left leg, a contusion of the left elbow
and a fracture of the jaw. O. E. Alger,
the engineer, was afterward arrested and
charged "with battery. He was subse
quently released on his own recogniz
ance by order of Judge Joachimsen.
According to several eye-witnesses of
the accident, McDonald was walking
along the outer track on Channel street
when, in attempting to get out of the
way of a freight train, he ran in the way
of the incoming train. Fortunately the
train was going at a slow rate of speed,
otherwise he would have been killed.
COMMISSIONERS ARE THROUGH.
City Hall Will Come Formally Into
the Supervisors' Hands To-Day.
The mission of the City Hall Commis
sioners is almost finished. The last roof
ing that was covered over the big pile ex
hausted the available funds, and to-day
the Commissioners are ready to turn tho
building over to the Board of Supervisors
and San Francisco will come formally
into possession of its own.
Since the passage of the law authoriz
ing the construction of the building over
$6,000,000 has been spent In its construc
tion, and local history tells that a goodly
proportion of that sum is represented in
numerous San Francisco fortunes rather
than in the building itself.
The hall is not yet built to the full in
tent of the original specifications, but the
Commissioners have decided that It is not
Just the right time to go to the Legisla
ture for more money, and the building
will be turned over as.lt is.
CANNED GOODS FOR MONTANA.
First Shipment of California Prod
ucts Is Sent North.
A train of fourteen cars left Oakland
Mole yesterday for Montana with canned
goods from the packing-house of Fontana
& Co. of this city. The goods were
shipped to the order of Walsh & Croft at
Butte. Before sending the order Walsh &
Croft corresponded with their customers
in the nortli and ascertained from thorn
the prospect of disposing of the consign
ment, which Includes peaches, pears,
plums, grapes, blackberries, cherries,
etc., put up in two-and-a-half pound cans
and In gallon cans, and an assortment of
choice jams and jellies.
Should this consignment of California
canned goods be .received with favor a
new market will pc ppened for the prod
ucts of the State.
WILL DOFF THE UNIFORM.
The Sixth California Regiment to Be
Mustered Out This Morning.
The Sixth Regiment of California
Volunteers, located at For^ Point, will be
mustered out of the Federal service to
day between the hours of 11 in the morn
ing and 2 in the afternoon. The Sixth
was the first California regiment to be
mustered into Uncle Sam's service, as it
the first to be mustered out.
A banquet was given Captain Henry C.
Danes, the mustering out officer, yester
day afternoon by his subordinate officers
at the officers' mess at Fort Point. The
affair was strictly military, but from the
expressed appreciation of many who at
tended the menu deviated considerably
from hard tack and coffee.
At the conclusion of the banquet an in
teresting address was delivered by Lieu
tenant Colonel W. R. Johnson.

HAD A WINE APPETITE
Frank Ricardo Charged With Swindling a
Number of Liquor Dealers,
FRANK RICARDO, whose
appetite for champagne
caused him to be arrested
on several charges of
misdemeanor, still lan
guishes In a cell in the City
Prison. Yesterday his case
was called in the Police Court,
and again continued. It is said
that other and more serious
charges will be placed against
Ricardo in a few days. His
alleged schc-me was to tele
phone to some grocer, and
after representing that he was
connected with the Press Club
or some other well-known or
ganization, order a case of
champagne. He would remain
outside of the club building
until the grocer or his clerk
showed up with the wine, and
after signing for it inform his
victim that he would settle the
bill the following day. Ricardo
would then take the wine to
his room, where he and his
friends would drink it. He
became so bold in his opera
tions that the police were no
tified and a concerted effort made to catch him.
(Mi November 17 Goldberf?. Bowen & Co. received a message from
the Cosmos Club for a sudplv of wine. Suspecting that the message
came from Ricardo, they at once telephoned to the California-street
station for an officer. The wine was given to a messenger boy to be
delivered, and accompanied by the policeman he started for the
headquarters of the club.
In front of the building was Ricardo waiting for the boy. He was
promptly placed under arrest and taken to police headquarters.
Since then the police have ascertained that he has worked at least a
dozen Srma in the same manner.
Another scheme of his was to telephone to a well-known club,
and after representing that he was a certain member, order a number
of bottles of wine sent to his room. He successfully worked this
scheme on several clubs.

As a rule some very warm things In
the racing line are unloaded in umbrella
time at the tracks, and between splashes
the crowd at Ingleside yesterday indulged
in some fancy gambling. The going was
sloppy, but not until the final number did
the expected long shot come to life. Then
Cavallo, with George Snider up, tripped
in first, backed from 30 down to 10. Four
of the other events fell to favorites in the
betting.
The Palace Hotel handicap for timber
toppers over the short steeplechase course
was the special attraction offered. It was
a noisy and very nervous betting thing.
Reno, this time ridden by T. Murphy, the
best of Andy Blakeley's aggregation,
finally closed favorite and won after mak
ing all the running. San Carlos was a
good second. Huntsman came to grief at
one of the jumps, shaking Shepard up
badly. While he was recovering the ani
mal was remounted by J. Boyd and took
third money. Jazebel was heavily played, i
but at the far corner jump both horse and
Jockey, Ellison, came to grief. It was a
sad accident; both escaped without in
jury. The more we have of these steeple
chase arrangements the more popular rac
injj will become in Manila when ti.at
country becomes civilized from a sporting
standpoint.

CHAMPIONSHIP GOES
TO BELMONT'S TEAM
The Lowell High School Succumbs
After a Hard Game Fought Out
in the Mud and Rain.
Belmont G, Lowell 0.
In mud and diminutive lakes the Bel
mont eleven wrested a victory from the
I,owells yesterday and thereby decided
the championship of the Amateur Aca
demic league for the football season of
IS9B. All the enthusiasm that the stu
dents of either institution could get to
gether was bottled up, carried to Recre
ation Park and let loose from time to
time In yells and whoops and screechings
unholy.
The mud of the gridiron wast of a soft,
oozing, mucilaginous consistency, which
soon destroyed all differences of facial
characteristics and left the players brown
plastered individuals. Tearing down tne
field the Belmonters by a ska,te or two
in the mud and by a stiff sidestroke
through the lakes carried the ball to
Lowell's one-yard line. But this was as
far as - Belmont went. A fumble at the
most critical moment . gave the pigskin
to the San Franciscans and it was kicked
out of danger. Neither side scored in the
first half.
In the second half the fierce playing be
gan.' Both teams wanted victory from the
soles of their wet feet to the crowns of
their mud-caked heads, and heavy marsh
and sea fighting was the order of the
battle. Back and forth the rushers ragei
until Belmont had the ball on the five
yard line of Lowell. Again were they
held and the High School boys got the
ball. It was passed to Hooper for a punt
out of danger. Hooper's drive was low
and the Belmont rushers broke through
blocking the kick. A mass of human mud
fell on the ball and Belmont had the only
touchdown of the day. Story kicked &
goal and the score read 6âo, at which it
stood until the close of the game.

THE EMPIIU. GUN CLUB.
Sportsmen Who Have Been Elected
to Office â Good Shooting
on the Preserve.
The Empire Gun Club Is now enjoying
the best shooting of any gun club that is
located on the Sonoma marshes and the
members expect that the sport will be
greatly Improved by the much-needed
rain. The following letter from Its secre
tary speaks for itself:
To the Sporting Editor of The Callâ My Dear
Sir: I beg to inform you that the election of
officers of the Empire Gun Club for the en
suing year, held on Tuesday evening last, re
sultrd In the following: President, A. M.
Shields: vice president. Dr. C K. Parent;
manager, J. Peltier; secretary. J. B. Haver;
sergeant at arms, T. J. I^ewis- enptin, J. I*.
Hazen; board of governors. Dr. F. Cornwall,
J. P. Sweeney and J. B. Haver.
I desire also to state that the shooting on our
preserves near Bla<*k Point is now very good
and that the sixteen members who visited the
grounds on Sunday last all returned with
satisfactory strings of birds. Mr. Durst being
the most successful, however, with a bag of
twenty-four. These were a mixed lot of teal,
mallard, sprig, canvasback and widgeon. Toura
very truly. J. B. HAUER.
Secretary Empire Gun Club.

THE WAR STAMP TflX CASES,
IF United States Circuit Judge Morrow desires to ren
tier the public a service in connection with the war
revenue stamp cases, which were submitted to him
en Tuesday, he will not let them get tangled up with
the application of the Attorney General to the local
c>u:!> for a writ of mandate. As in all cases where
attempts have been made to force the express compa
pay their war taxes by mandamus, the pro
ceeding of the Attorney General involves jurisdic
tional questions entirely foreign to the main issue.
! thoc suits have actually been dismissed
without ever reaching the merits of the litigation.
The Attorney General has already been turned down
by the State Supreme Court, and his application for
mandate to the Superior Court, having been trans
ferred to Judge Morrow's tribunal, cannot in the na
ture of things uive rise to anything but a debate over
whether or not the Federal courts can issue man
damus to compel the performance of duties enjoined
by State laws. While this is going on the public is
paying the war tax and the express companies are
paying attorney^ fees.
We do not positively assert that the people are be
ing robbed in this matter, but five courts have al
ready so decided, and the Attorney General of the
United States, one of his assistants, and the Com
missioner of Internal Revenue, have agreed' with
them. What the people now want Judge Morrow to
do is to tell them frankly whether or not he thinks all
these gentlemen have taken a correct view of the law.
The people have great faith in his legal learning, in
tegrity and perspicacity, and whatever he says upon
the subject, irrespective of what the Gircuit Court of
Appeals may say, will go far toward satisfying them.
In other words, the people look to Judge Morrow
to decide the question submitted in the Benhatn and
Johnson cases immediately.
Whatever the result, probably an appeal will be
taken, but Judge Morrow need not consider that. The
controverted point has been placed squarely before
him in the cases referred to, and the litigants are en
titled to the benefit of his opinion. If it shall turn
cut that in his judgment the patrons of Wells, Fargo
& Co. are required by law to pay this war tax. they
will have the satisfaction of knowing that the subject
has received the careful consideration of another con
scientious jurist, entirely free from embarrassing law
points and jurisdictional questions.
There is another point in connection with the
mingling of the Bcnham and Johnson cases with the
Attorney General's application for mandate which
should be considered. Mr. Fitzgerald retires from
office on January 2. and his successor. Mr. Ford, may
take an altogether different view of the law. He may
conclude that mandate is not the remedy and aban
don the ca=e. Judge Morrow should not await the
event of any such contingency as this. If the war
revenue law requires Wells. Fargo & Co. to pay this
tax. the people of Calfornia are being robbed of up
ward of $10,000 a month, and it is important that a
conclusion should be reached as speedily as possible.
When a man's pocket is being picked he is anxious to
know whether or not the process is legal. The
fleeced patrons of the express company will sleep bet
ti r after Judge Morrow rules, even though he rule
against them.
MR. MAGUIRE'S LONG RANGE LETTER
JUDGE MAGUIRE has written a very longi
tudinal letter to Father Vorkc, who is many
meridians of longitude distant, on the subject
of the hitter's appearance in the recent campaign.
All that Judge Majxuire puts in this too. too long
letter is summed up in the statement that it is to ius
ti;y his book on Ireland and the Pope, in which he
calls the Pope "the serpent of the Vatican" because
he forbade Irish priests to take a part in poli
tics. Maguire contended that the priests should be
free to go into politics. But in the same letter he
roundly abuses Father Yorke for going into politics,
and says. "I have a feeling, and a strong feeling,
against clerical interference in politics."
From all of this tiresome torrent of words we arc
only able io gather that Judge Maguire believes in
clerical interference in politics when it is for his side,
but when it is against him it is an outrage.
As he has lined up with the Pope in deprecating
political interference in politics, let him take to him
self also the reptilian character he ascribed to Leo
XIII and then quiet himself.
The Chronicle publishes an excellent picture of
somebody and labels it "General Brooke." As it
represents a good-looking man, Brooke has no rea
son for complaint.
So many young women jump from ferry-boats as
to suggest that the crew keep watch and essay to
cherk the manifest tendency to climb the rail.
THE SA]S" FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, DECEMBER 15, 1898.
TbJE VfILUE OF THE PHILIPPINES.
OX December 2 this news telegram was sent
from Vancouver, B. C: "Advices from New
South Wales say that the steamer Culgon has
sailed from .Sydney with a cargo of provisions for
Rear Admiral Dewey's ileet at Manila. The cargo
consists of 5000 carcasses of mutton, 250 of lambs, 81
tons of onions, 125 tons of potatoes and 22 tons of
carrots."
This reminds the country not only of the incapacity
of the Philippines to furnish a food supply for our
navy and garrisons, but that the islands belong to a
different and distant system and lie nearer to other
productive countries than to us. This supply of food
for our navy is the first expansion of commerce felt
since we demanded possession of that archi
pelago, but it does not expand the trade of
San Francisco. Its benefits go to Sydney and
to the stock men and farmers of Australia,
while our stock men and farmers pay the taxes which
buy the cargo. The New York Tribune, commenting
on it. says: "The Philippines are 7000 miles away,
far nearer to other great powers or their possessions
than to us, and belonging to the commercial and
geographical system of another continent."
Their conquest presents features repugnant to
American sentiment and antagonistic to American
prosperity. What they produce in sugar, hemp, rice
and tnbncco enters into direct competition with the
production of American farmers, while climate and
distance seem to forbid that American farmers shall
supply ever, the army and navy stores needed to feed
tlic large force by land and sea which must watch and
tend them.
If we can force labor in the cane fields of the Phil
ippines as Spain did, those fields can readily crush
out sugar production at home. What that will mean
may be estimated by a study of our beet sugar pro
duction in the United States.
There are over twenty beet sugar factories in this
country, which produce this year 120,000,000 pounds
of refined sugar. In 1894 only five of these were in
operation, and their production was only 44,886.000
pounds. The industry has flourished and advanced
here faster than in any other country where it has
been established. In 1889 its production was only
4.000,000 pounds; the increase since then is 116.000,
000 pounds, in nine years.
It took France twenty years to reach a production
of 100.000,000 pounds of beet sugar. We have exceeded
it in nine years. This has been done by free, white, vol
untary labor in the fields and factories. It has added a
valuable crop for rotation to American farms.
The Republican platform of 1896 said: "We con
demn the Democratic administration for not keeping
faith with the sugar producers of the United States.
The Republican party favors such pr6tection as will
lead to the production on American soil of all the
sugar which the American people use, and for which
we are sending abroad annually more than $100,000,
000 to foreign countries."
The soil of the United States in 1896 was conti
nental soil. The labor to produce thereon all the
sugar consumed by our people was American white
labor, paid at white rates.
That party pledge is not kept by going forth on the
altruistic warpath and coming home loaded with spoils
in the shape of tropical islands, which can produce
every pound of sugar we use, which can do this with
the involuntary labor of the tropics, paid on a scale
that would starve voluntary white labor at home.
Well may the American farmer and laborer ask
what stake they have in imperialism when it obsoletes
the use of. their land and destroys the wages of their
labor, without even the compensating advantage of
furnishing mutton and potatoes to our imperial mili
tary establishment, required to hold our new posses
sions in subjection.
Perhaps no part of the country is hit harder by this
policy than California. The peculiarities of our cli
mate limit annual crops for rotation. We have much
soil adapted to the sugar beet that is not fitted for
other annual crops until beet culture has subdued its
refractory elements. The beet came to us as a reve
lation and blessing. Wherever sugar factories have
been planted community life has taken on a fresh im
pulse, lands have acquired a new value, commerce
has extended its operations and its profits, and the
commonwealth has gained. If all this be canceled
those responsible for its destruction will have a day
of settlement with the people.
OUR WINE IN MEXICO.
WE publish this morning a story from the Two
Republics of the City of Mexico to the effect
that thirty-three barrels of California wine
consigned to a dealer in that city have been seized re
cently by the Government officials and condemned on
the ground that the wine contained an admixture of
salicylic acid, which the Mexican authorities regard
as dangerous to health.
The story is given by the Two Republics with con
siderable detail and need not be repeated here. It
sufiices to say that the dealer in whose hands the wine
was seizedj makes no complaint of unfairness on the
part of the officials, but, on the contrary, declares that
an analysis made at his own instigation after the
seizure confirms the report of the officials and proves
that they were right in condemning the wine.
It is to be noted that the Two Republics adds that
the dealer who suffers in this case is not "the first
victim in Mexico of the contemptible business prac
ticed by California shippers in doctoring young wines
with salicylic acid." It gives the name of another
wine importing house that had a similar experience
about seven months ago, though not to so great an
extent as in the recent case.
This is a severe charge to be made against our wine
shippers, and it cannot be too promptly investigated.
The assertion that some of our dealers make a "prac
tice" of doctoring young wines with salicylic acid,
coming from a journal of such circulation as the Two
Republics, will seriously injure our trade unless it is
refuted, or the dishonest dealers so thoroughly ex
posed that they can no longer injure the general wine
trade of the State.
The importance of the issue can hardly be over
rated. Our wine industry is an expanding one. and
there is urgent need for an expanding market. Mex
ico and the other Spanish republics to the south of
us offer good opportunities for such a market, as
their populations are large consumers of wine. To
sacrifice that market for pure wine for the sake of
lotting some dishonest dealers make a profit out of
doctored wines would be a commercial blunder of the
first magnitude.
The issue is another phase of the old subject of pure
food laws. California is so large a producer of the
class of food articles known as luxuries, such as wine,
fruit preserves and olive oil, that she is seriously in
jured by every sort of food adulteration, and; unfor
tunately, their name is legion. A multitude of oils,
preserves, jellies, jams and wine, adulterated with
every mixture that cunning can devise arid greed make
use of, are sent abroad bearing California labels.
Against these our producers of pure foods and wines
have to contend, and the contest is a hard one.
However difficult it may be to deal with the prob
lem as a whole, it seems clear that in the special case
reported by the Two Republics something might be
done. The article gives the names of the dealer in the
City of Mexico and the shipper from California. The
opportunity for an investigation is therefore open.
The State Board of Trade, the Manufacturers' and
Producers' Association, and the two associations
representing the wine industry and trade of the State,
should see if they cannot devise some system of pre
venting such wrongs as that complained of and pro
tect the credit of our wines in the Mexican market.
BEYOND THE REACH OF LOW.
EVERY day intensifies the belief that in the
School Board are men who .are rogues, whose
proper place would be the penitentiary, and
fitting garb the stripes of felons. The wantonness
and brazen impudence of their sinning cause aston
ishment to mingle with indignation, for they go about
unmolested, and in their faces is seen no blush of
shame. They act as those having no fear of conse
quences.
Perhaps this latter condition of mind is nothing
strange. When a set of men elected to high office de
liberately and systematically begin robbing the com
munity which had trusted them; when the result of
their villainy is made palpable through depleted funds,
unpaid teachers, schools suffering for supplies: when
there are authentic tales of bribery and plain evidence
of corruption in other directions, it would appear at
first glance that the people would be given an oppor
tunity at least of securing the poor redress of seeing
punishment meted out.
There is no sign that the recreant Directors will be
made to suffer nor to disgorge their loot. The Grand
Jury, a body organized for the discouragement of vice,
tackled the godless outfit, questioned, probed and
dropped the matter. Now we are gravely informed
that an indictment under the one-twelfth act would
not stand. Even so. are there no laws against theft?
Is not bribery a crime? Is conspiracy to rob no
offense to be dealt with? The public may accept as a
sorry fact the condition that this rotten board is to
escape punishment, but it will decline to accept it with
â an approving smile, or to believe that a larcenous
School Director is more entitled to immunity than is
the footpad, the burglar or the pickpocket, either one
of whom, as a member of the present board, would be
in a congenial atmosphere, cheek by jowl with those
of his own kind.
NOSTALGIA IN ThfE PHILIPPINES.
WITH signing of articles of peace there is no
doubt the lingering glamour of war departed.
The boys who sailed from this port last
June shouting their enthusiasm are homesick now.
They sigh lor the cool, green hills of California, the
breezes sweeping in from the sea, the banks of fo.g
billowing in through the Golden Gate.
Nobody can blame them. They are in an unlovely
land, where the climate is laden with malady, the food
not adapted to the needs of people of a temperate
growth, the work hard and onerous, devoid of glory,
remuneration or hope. They feel that they have done
their full duty. One of the soldiers, an ardent and
patriotic American, among the first to enlist, and who,
with the other brave boys, faced Spanish fire and did
not flinch, writes to a friend on the staff of this paper:
"Get my discharge and transportation HOME.
Army life in the piping times of peace is several dis
tinct and well-defined kinds, sorts and conditions of
hell."
Without doubt this .represents the general feeling,
and, as it does so. there cannot be haste too great in
sending regulars to the relief of the volunteers. For
the most part the regulars are in some measure sea
soned. They are commanded by experienced officers,
and have acquired the knack of making the best of
every situation. Moreover, few of them have gone
into the service under the impulse of sentiment. To
them the army is a business and a means of liveli
hood They would go without repining wherever or
dered, and stay without regret, while their peril from
disease would be minimum. When men are thor
oughly homesick the ailment becomes physical. They
are unfitted for resisting actual disease, and they lose
ambition.
Perhaps a little brush with Aguinaldo would cheer
the boys up, but in the absence of a definite prospect
cf this, the most cheering message possible would be
one calling them back where friends and royal wel
come wait their coming.
LESSON OF THE NEW YORK FIRE.
AS a result of the great fire in New York, in
which were involved two tall buildings â those
of the Home Life Insurance Company and of
the Postal Telegraph Company â there has been an
extensive discussion in the East as to the comparative
advantages and disadvantages of the modern sky
scraper in case of fire.
The test to which the tall structwes were submitted
in New York was an extraordinary one. The fire
started in a five-story building which was full of in
flammable material. The wind was blowing a tre
mendous gale, equal to a speed of sixty miles an hour,
and the flames from the burning building were car
ried directly, with all the fierceness of a blast furnace,
against the walls of the Home Life. It appears the
windows of the Home Life were not provided with
iron shutters, and the flames were driven through
them to the interior woodwork. Yet the building
stood, the walls were not affected, and the iron work
was apparently not injured sufficiently to require any
considerable rebuilding. The Postal Telegraph build
ing escaped with very little damage.
The day after the fire the Chief of the Fire Depart- j
ment was quoted as having stated that no building !
should be higher than 150 feet, as it is impossible to j
protect tall structures, bnt on the following day, after \
a full examination, the Chief of the Building Depart
ment of the city stated that if it had not been for the '
two tall buildings the fire would have swept the whole i
block as far south as St. Paul's Church.
The discussion that has followed shows that the
weight of authority agrees with the statement of the
Chief of the Building Department. Indeed the con
clusion seems to be that the Home Life was not an
entirely up-to-date structure, and that, had it been so,
it would have stopped the flames at once. The tall
building seems, in fact, to be a protector to all the
property around it. It prevents the spread of flames
and localizes any fire that breaks out adjoining it. The
consensus of Eastern authorities on the subject is
that a tall building, modern in every respect, is vir
tually fireproof, and, as the Philadelphia Inquirer says,
"to destroy one of them, judging -by the New York
fire, would be next to impossible."
One of the Botkin witnesses from Delaware has
been frightened by the loud tones of Attorney Knight,
and not unnaturally. But in California that voice has
lest its power to intimidate and subdue.
There is not so much evidence that the Chicago
Aldermen experienced a moral reform as that the idea
of devoting their unhallowed persons to the decora
tion of lampposts was not pleasing
A GREAT INDUSTRY MENACED
The Two Republics, a newspaper published in the English language In
the City of Mexico, and having an extensive circulation, contrv â ' in Its
issue of December 6 a story to the effect that thirty-three barrels of Cali
fornia wine consigned to a dealer in that city have been seized by the official
wine inspectors on the ground that the wine contained salicylic acid, a sub
stance regarded by the Mexican authorities as dangerous to health.
As the story was published in the Two Republics it is safe to say it has
been published also in most, if not all, the Mexican journals, and it goes
without saying that the incident will be used by wine dealers of other
countries to discredit California wine in the Mexican market. The issue is,
therefore, one of vast importance to the State, and particularly so to all
persons engaged in the wine industry, whether as producers or dealers.
The great interests at stake justify a thorough investigation of the sub
ject by the State Board of Trade and the two associations that represent
the wine producers and wine dealers of California. We invite their atten
tion to the story, which as published in the Two Republics is as follows:
Thirty-three barrels of California wine consigned to Federlco Navarro, the
well known and popular young commission merchant and importer, have Just been
seized by the wine gaugers employed by the City Health Department on tne
ground that the wine was found to contain salicylic acid, a substance declared oy
Mexican authority to be dangerous to health. And that is not all the miscnier
which the recklessness of the Californians who shipped the wine have done Mr.
Navarro, for his customers are returning him all the same kind of wine he has
sold them before, in order to avoid seizure of the same on their premises and nne
and imprisonment for having adulterated wine in their stock of merchandise.
These returns amount to from one to ten barrels in each case. A few or Jir.
Navarro's customers have indeed already fallen under the law by Having stocK
purchased from Navarro seized. They are all looking of course to him tor re
dress. The measure taken by the authorities is stern, but entirely just, and only
the natural result or' the recklessness of the California shippers, who are in tne
habit of adding salicylic acid to very young wine to age it in order to be able to
class it as a higher grade. .
Mr. Navarro was seen last evening by a representative of the Two Repub
lics and freely admitted that the authorities had done nothing but what was
right in the premises, as the analysis made since the seizure by his own instiga
tion fully concurred with that of the health officers, and the wine should never
have been brought to Mexico.
"I associated myself," he stated, "with a couple of young business men in L.os
Angeles. Cal., for the importation of wines from that locality into Mexico, reel
ing convinced that with the display of push and energy a very good market could
be built up for the California product. We were just beginning to reap some
profit from the venture when It seems the exporter from whom we bought in
the best of faith conceived the diabolical idea to increase hia profits by J-h'P
ping us a doctored article instead of the pure article to which we were entitled
as we agreed to pay a higher price than is paid for any California wine brojgnt
to this city In carioad lots. The shipper in this case is Mr. "vV oollacott, and the
wrong he has caused is almost irretrievable, as he has not only cast discredit
upon the California wine business but also exposes myself and others to \er>
serious consequences. The law regarding adulterated articles is very strict in
Mexico. It does not only provide for the seizure and destruction of the adulter
ated articles but also imposes heavy money fines and imprisonment upon tne
unlucky merchant who offers the stuff for sale, not taking into consideration
whether or not the respective merchant was aware of the adulteration. 1 trust
that Mr. Woollacott is not personally responsible for the adulteration and that
he can make a satisfactory explanation of the fraud which has evidently been
committed, but thero is no doubt as to his responsibility from every standpoint
for the great wrong committed and thn costly damage done. I had just settled
the freight and duties on the shipment, amounting to $2120, which is, of course a
total loss. Upward of $2600 of outstanding accounts cannot be collected now be
cause the people who owe them are returning to me all the stock left on their
hands. Others who do not owe are returning their stocks also and are looking to
me to make good their losses. The value of the wine confiscated amounts to
about $3500. Of course I cannot now ostimatethe money damage this whole busi
ness has caused, but it is very considerable."
Mr Navarro is not the llrst victim in Mexico of the contemptible business
practiced by California shippers in doctoring young wines with salicylic acid.
Carlos Hirachberg head of one of the oldest wine importing houses in the city,
had a similar experience about seven months ago, though not on so large a scale,
as the seizure extended to only a few barrels, a sample shipment, and he was able
to stop a carload that was on its way. In that case the wine had been bought
from a sample which had been analyzed and found to be absolutely pure. Mr.
Hirschberg pot off with the payment of wines for himself and several custom
ers, whose poods bought from him had been seized, by showing his own absolute
innocence and that the shipper alone was to blame.
It is very much to be hoped not alone for the sake of Mr. Navarro but also
in the Interest of American trade with Mexico that Mr. Woollacott or whoever
else has been guilty of defraudinK Mr. Navarro and his customers by shipping
doctored instead of pure wines will make all restitution in his power, and that the
California win* 1 shippers will adopt some sort of system to protect their cus
tomers and their trade against the infamous fraud that has been practiced in
the present case.
The seizure has extended only to clarets, the Zlnfandels, RelsllnÂ«R, Muscatels
and Angelicas imported by Mr. Navarro being free from any admixture.

for schoolmates and ttacTiers.

sembled friends with an impromptu pro

First Test of the New Opposition
Company Made Successfully.
The first test of the Equitable Gaslight
Company, the new corporation that has
been founded for the purpose of compet
ing with the San- Francisco I Gaslight and

afternoon ' at the headquarters of the

rived to patrons of it was complete in
every detail, and was made before the
prominent business men of the 'commu
nity and many other invited guests. By
an improved* process the new company
can supply its subscribers with illumin
ating gas at a uniform price of $1 a thou
sand, cubic feet, which is 50 p,er cent less
than that now paid to the San Francisco
Gas and Electric . Company. In addition
to this reduction in price the gas sup
plied is far superior to that now used by
the public, as it is thoroughly purified
by improved machinery before it is al
lowed to : enter the mains traversing the
various sections of the city. '
In the process of manufacture gas is
used almost exclusively. It is burned in
the furnaces and afterward extracts it
self, as it were, from the coal.
As an exhibition of the quality of the
gas that will bo supplied to the public in
the near future at a rate much below
that now paid, a number of the various
lamps and burners supplied and used by
the San Francisco Gaslight Company
were placed for inspection before the vis
itors. The light as shown is > far supe
rior to that seen around the city both ii<
brightness and steadiness. The im
proved burners made specially to produce
a brighter and fuller light now supplied
to stores, etc., by the San Francisco Com
pany were also tested with perfect satis
faction. Where the patrons of the old Las
company pay an exorbitant price for the
use of gas through these burners the new
company makes a reduction in their us^
of j almost 50 per cent on the price now

THE EMPEROR'S
RAPID UNDOING
Advocated Customs of
White Men.
NOW HELD A ROYAL PRISONER
RUSSIA IS ASSUMING CONTROL
OF THE CHINESE EMPIRE.
Grave Problems Now Confronting
England and America and a
Big War May Soon
Be On.
Among the^rurstr at the Occidental who
arrived on the seamer City of Peking,
is O. i"nx. He i om< from China
registered from Peking. Mr. Cox is
on Englishman and was b irn In Austra
lia, but hi -' thirteen
years In China, lie is now on his way
i never having y.et visited
that country. Mr. Cox's connections in
\vÂ«n- such that he is In a position
t" be exceptionally well Informed regard
ing the* affairs of that country, and es
pecially is ho well informed concerning
the International question of interest in
China. He talks in an exceedingly modest
manner. His version or the recent over
throw of the Emperor is best told in his
own words. Regarding this he said:
"The Emperor has not been murdered.
He i^ simply held a prisoner as it were
by his mother. He is confined in the
royal i alace. There Is no doubt but that
his mother usurped the reins of govern
ment, for the Emperor did not abdicate.
To me it seems strange that the other
nations recognize her as the lawful ad
ministrator of the affairs of the nation,
when they know that there has been no
formal abdication on the part of the Era
peror. The latter undoubtedly brought
about his downfall because of the radical
reform measures that he advocated. Many
of the high officials of the empire were in
the reform move with the Emperor, and it
might have been successful, but some one
of them, with the usual duplicity .of the
j race, gave the scheme away and the
Emperor's mother took immediate charge
and made things hot for the reformers.
â¢â¢The Emperor wanted to introduce
Western customs. He advocated the
wearing apparel of the white man and he
Treated his subjects to dock their queues,
and this was more than the Chinese
would stand for. Chang Yin Huan, for
mer Minister to the Court of St. James,
and who also attended the Queen's Jubilee j
where he was knighted, was one of the
reformers, and but lor the intervention of
Sir Claud Mac Donald he would have lost i
his head. As it was he was banished !
from the empire. Another high official
escaped by taking refuge on a British
warship. Tnere is no doubt but that, these
: prising reformers would have made
a clean sweep if their plans had succeed
ed. I believe that their zeal would have
led th^m to a free use of the ax, but their
following was not strong enough, so they j
failed.
"The most serious and at the same time !
tho most interesting question to the Eng
lish and Americans in China at present
i.s the attitude of Russia. There is no use
denying the fact or trying to disguise
the stern reality that Russia practically
controls the Chinese Government. This
must lead to international dissensions,
an*i I look to see in the next two years
a his war between England and Russia.
Something has to be done, for if affairs go
on as they have been Russia will control
the city of Peking in two years. England
and America are not looking after their !
interests as they should. Russia controls j
Xowchwang, a treaty port, which is 1
within 200 miles of Peking. In the district
of Manchuria, in the northern part of Chi
na. Russia is building railways. This is
the richest and most desirable part of
- China. While America and England are
' standing by looking on Russia is daily In
creasing her strength. From the port of
Newchwang America gets at least 70 per |
cent of Its China trade. Can this country
afford to allow Russia to control this i
port?
"I am not prepared to say what Eng
land and America combined could do In a \
war with Russia, It would be a hard ;
country to fight. America has no army, i
I admit you can raise a large volunteer
force, but you can't make soldiers in a |
day out of volunteers. Russia has her
best forces in China, and they have got to i
be met by men who have besides mere
bravery the knowledge of how to be sol
diers. The question is a grave and serious
one. and it is being carefully considered
in England. What will be the outcome I
1

PUGILISTS IN TOWN.
"Cocker" Tweedie Has Returned
From Australia â La Fontaine
and Tye Matched.
"Cocker" Tweedie, the Australian
lightweight pugilist, who was defeated
at one of the Olympic Club entertain
ments by Jimmy Lawlor, returned yes
terday from the land of the golden
fleece.
Tweedie iÂ« very anxious to have an
opportunity of proving to the sporting
people of this city that he Is a much
better man pugilistically than many of
the local sporting men suppose. He
says that it was through carelessness
on his part that he was whipped by
Lawlor. He is ready now to sign
articles with any man of his weight in
America for a limited contest or a con
test to a finish.
The National Athletic Club hap signed
â¢Charley Tye, the clever pugiiiat of the
Reliance Club of Oakland, and La Fon
taine, a French-Canadian fighter, who
; arrived in this city recently, to box
ten rounds in Woodward's Pavilion on
the evening of the Green-Creedon
i twenty-round contest.
La Fontaine is said to be a very stiff
puncher and a game fellow, who has
won many har*-fought battles. Tye
will train in Oakland and his opponent
in Alameda.

Compromised Their Claims.
The numerous architects who sub
mitted plans for the proposed City and
County Hospital have at last com
promised their claims. Each will receive
as compensation for his services, if the
Board of Supervisors will pass such a
resolution, the sum of $375. This .amount,
however, will not buy the plans. These
will be donated to th^ city free of all
cost. The city will either retain them for
three months or hold thorn permanently.
This arrangement was practically arrived
at yesterday when Supervisor Dodge,
chairman of the Hospital Committee, met
with the architects in the committee
room. The discussion was long and heat
ed, and a few of the architects did not
agree to tm- plan proposed, but it is
thought they will come round before the
final meeting is held on the matter.

Fell From a Scaffold.
Yesterday morning at 10 o'clock, while
Harry Hagerman. a carpenter, was mak
ing some repairs in Montague's water pipe
factory, corner of Townsend and Sixth
streets, the scaffold on which lie was
I working gave way. precipitating him to
; the ground, a distance of sixteen feet.
I He landed on an iron pipe, breaking two
ribs. Hagerman was taken to his home
at 2?.2r, Mission street and medical asaist
j ance secured. He will be removed to St.
Mary's Hospital this morning.

WHO CAN KNOCK A "CHINK"
THE GREATER DISTANCE?
The Question Which Has Precipitated a Fierce War
â¢ Eetween the Members cf the Two Police
Squads m Chinatown.
THE war of the highbinders is not the only one being waged In China
town. The police sent up into the Mongolian quarter to put a stop to
strife are at swords' points each with the other. They have organized
their tongs, | ihelr knives, put now handles in their
hammers, and each officer Is a member of i separate knockers' club, the
object of which Is to <!â ; up some brother oil
The fn-een-eyed monster is th>- cause of the war. in which such venom
and bitterness is displayed that it bios fair to exceed in fierceness vie
battlo between the hatchettnen. The question which is agitating the po
lice is which cf th<-m is doing the most work. Each claims that no
one is working but himself, ajid that all the honor due for tlie existing
peace in Chinatown belongs to him. Each confides to his particular friend
that be can smash a door in quicker than his mates, and that when it comes
to knocking down a luckless highbinder found in a raided den the others
c;ii;nut come near him irs the precision with which he lands his blow on
:i of the Celestial's face, and the distance which the
"t hink" la huri'-.i by the force of hip Rood right swing.
itenante Price and Eeola (or ISsola and Price) have charge of the
special Chinatown Bquad. Which la senior in command is In doubt. When
the two lieutenants meet it la rumored that the atmosphere in their neigh
borhood congeals ami huge Icicles form between them. The forded friend
liness exhibited is amusing- to the initiated. Profound bows and Balaams
are given and returned, and one rot knowing would think that "Bill" and
"Fred" *re old cronies, but they are not.
When the Suey Bing-Suey ring trouble Started some months ago Detec
tives Gibson and Wren and Patrolman George McMahon were detailed to
keep peace among the highbinders, but the murders continued. Chief Leos
became wrathy, and being determined that the shooting should be stopped
placed an additional squu.il on duty. This did not exactly tickle the palates of
the three officers :irs! on the ground and there is a decided coolness exist
ing between them, the lieutenants and the members of their posse.
Sergeant McManua also has a thorn in his side. He has regarded
Chinatown as his particular hunting ground, and resents the presence of
the new posse, which Is poaching OH his preserves. It is rumored that the
members of his squad are not exactly satisfied with the sergeant, and that
there is also a split between the officers under him. With but few excep
tions the police on the special squad are bo unfriendly that they hardly
speak to each other.
While this bickering is going: on among the officers of the law the wily
Mongolian smileÃ. Peace reigns at present, but it is only because the
highbinders are not ready to strike. Hatchets are being sharpened and
pistols loaded. When the time is ripe the weapons will be used, and, aid
ed by the jealousy existing among the members of the Police Department,
the hatchetmen hope to escape detection.

THEY WANT THE
MINERAL LANDS
THROWN OPEN
Plans of the Miners'
Association.
13,000,000 ACRES INVOLVED
ALL NOW CLASSED AS '-AGRI
CULTURAL."
The Idaho-Montana Law desired :a
This Stateâ lt Would Bring
Activity and Pros
per, ty.
The proper disposition of the mineral
lands of California that are now held
as agricultural lands is a subject that is
particularly engaging the attention of
th<? California .miners' Association at
the present time. The vast area ( f lands
bearing minerals of various kinds that
are embraced in the grants of the South
ern Pacific Company and others, and
which should be classified as open to the
enterprise of the miner, is the prize the
association is striving to win.
During the last two sessions of Con
| gres? a bill has been under consideration
; to put these lands under the same rules
and regulations as those in Idaho and
Montana, There the conditions are such
that the miner has equal rights with the
farmer or stock raiser, and the results
have proved highly beneficial to the par
ties concerned.
In this State the enterprise of the pros
pector is checked by the fact that if
ho finds a good lead on land classified
as agricultural he must be possessed of
means to go into court and fight for the
right to develop the prospect or abandon
It to his mere fortunate successor. If
the aims of the California Miners' Asso
ciation are accomplished all this handi
cap will be a voided and there will be a
great impetus given to the mining in
dustry.
"The people of California will better
understand this matter when it is known
that within the lines of our common
wealth there are 13,000,000 acres of land
that are now classified as agricultural
that should be open to the industry of tue
man with the pick and shovelâ the wealth
producer; the man who dug out of the
ground the sinews with which the war
of the rebellion was conducted to a suc
cessful conclusion." So stated Julian
Sonntag of the Miners' Association yes
The Peculiar Death of Gaetano
Aleoto, an Italian Fish*
erman.
GAETAXO ALEOTO, an Italian fisher
man, whose home was in Vallejo,
was found dead in the cabin of his
fishing smack near Fishermen's
wharf at half-past 6 o'clock yester
day morning. Carlo Quartarra, an
other fisherman, went on noard to awaken
Aleoto. He found tho hatch closed and
the little cabin stifling with the fumes
from a small charcoal furnace used to
warm the cabin. Aleoto was dead. He
had lighted tho fire and had closed the
hatch on the evening previous for the
purpose of keeping out the cold. Quar
tarra found that the fire had burned out,
and so had the life of Aleoto.
The dead man was ony 21 years old and
â was unmarried.
terday In discussing: the Importance of I
the measure now pending in Congress.
"It is the desire of the mining men of
this State," said he, "that the Govern
ment create a commission of competent
men to classify our lands that are now
In dispute and that the decision of such
commission should be final. This has
been done in Idaho and Montana and the
plan has worked admirably. We cannot
see why the same regulations should not
apply to similar lands in this State. Un
der the application of the Idaho-Mon
tana act the cost was between 3 and 4
cents per acre and there is no reason why
it should be more here. The measure hag
the support of the entire California del
egation in Congress and our organiza
tion is working diligently to assure its
passage. We do not feel confident that
we will succeed at this session, but there
Is little doubt that the bill will become
a law in the long session.
"There are fifteen mining States in this
country and we are confident of our abil
ity to swing every one Into line on this
proposition. Of course, this will take
time, but our interests are practically
identical and we are bound to get to
gether on the proposition to demand the
appointment of a Cabinet officer to at
tend solely to the affairs of mines and
mining. Other countries give to this in
dustry the consideration and support to
which it Is entitled, but the miners of the
united States have been very shabbily
treated in so far as recognition and pro
tection are concerned. Now is the time
to adjust these matters and if the news
papers will stand in with us we will suc
ceed.
"The importance of the mining industry
of this State is not appreciated by the
public. If it were there would be more I
effort to assure it just treatment. There I
re Â°ii n Q 7^ ed . in thls work in California
o\er 19.000 miners, every one of whom is
a consumer. On the one-half basis of the
Chicago census we will estimate popula
tion two to one. At a low estimate each
will consume 50 cents" worth of supplies
each day. This would mean $19,000 per
Jav .â v<?r a half million dollars a month
and between six and seven million dol
lars each year. If this mineral lands bill
goes through this number will be In
creased to 25.000 or 30.000 men, every one
of whom would be a factor in promoting
the welfare of other industries of the
Mate.
mn s }?r? r fea * ure of the situation is the <
modest fart that California mines pro
duced 5G0.000.000 this year which can be
accounted so much contributed 10 the
wealth of the State. With the classifi
cation of the mineral lands many preat
mines would be developed and this im- !
mense output largely Increased. We pro- i
pose to have this recognition and will
keep on fighting until we get it. If it is i
necessary the great copper, silver, oil, !
coal and iron interests can be combined. I
\\lth such an interest brought to bear on !
Congress there can be no doubt as to the I
result. At the same time there should be i
no necessity for such strife to secure rec
ognition for one of the most Important in
dustries of the country.
"We must have a department of mines
and mining in the place of the Interior
Department, which necessarily cannot I
give us the proper attention. In addition
to this the men interested in such enter- '
prises should have the benefit of an offi
cial geographical map, showing the loca
tion of mineral lands, quantity and qual
ity of timber, water supply and the means
of transportation to reach such lands.
Other countries encourage the industry
by such means, and there is no reason
why the United States should not do so. i
There is untold wealth lying in the moun- i
tains of California, and every due en
couragement ought to be given to the I
men who have the enterprise to dig it '
out and contribute to the wealth of the
nation."
The California Miners' Association
hopes to accomplish some good work in
the next Congress, one part of which wJll
be to impress the Government with the
necessity of dredging San Pablo Bay and !
the Sacramento River, which are being j
made shallow by the erosion of adjacent \
lands. If France and Italy had not ener
getically pursued such work their rivers
would now be dry land. It is only a ques- I
tion of a short time when our lawmakers
will appreciate the vast importance of
these matters to the country at large.
The association may find it necessary to
send an able man to Washington to Bet be
fore the coming Congress the necessity
of establishing the new branch of gov
ernment. â _

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy over the entire Pa
cific slope this evening, except in extreme
Northwestern California, where it is clear.
Rain has fallen generally over California as
far south as Los Angeles during the day and
will extend over the extreme southern portion I
of the State to-nicht and early Thursday
Imorning. It Is now raining at Fresno and Los
Angeles and snowing at Independence.
The pressure has risen over the northwestern
portion of the Pacific slope during the day
and fallen over the plateau region.
The temperature has fallen in Southern Call
1 mia and risen over the plateau region. Else
where it has remained about stationary.
Conditions are favorable for temporarily
clearing weather Thursday.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, December 15, IS9S:
Northern Californiaâ Partly cloudy Thursday;
warmer; light northerly wind.
Southern Californiaâ Generally cloudy Thurs
day, except rain in the southern portion Thurs
day morning; warmer; light northerly wind.
Nevada â Cloudy Thursday, with snow In the
mountains.
I'tahâ Cloudy Thursday, with snow in the
mountains of the south portion.
Arizonaâ Rain Thursday, with snow in the
mountains.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Partly cloudy
Thursday, probably becoming threatening
Thursday evening; light northeast wind;
warmer.
Special from Mount Tamalpaisâ Foggy; wind
north, 20 miles: temperature. 36; maximum. 42.
G. H. WILLSON, Local Forecast Official.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Dec. 14.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
There was less business In the market here to
day, but the tone was firm. All Interest cen
tered in Americans. They opened dull under
parity, traders being Inclined to take profits,
then rallied on New York purchases of coalers,
but later New York realized, causing a dull
finish. Spanish bonds were 43H on a report
that the coupon will be paid, 3 per cent in
gold. Money was harder. There was consid
erable from the bank at 4 per cent on three
days, in view of the impending demands.

| CHICAGO, Dec. 14.â At the opening and un-
I til after the noon hour wheat showed a good
| deal of firmness. Opening- was 'ie higher. May
advanced under good general buying to Q&%6
6G' 2 <\ Here realizing and some selling against
calls resulted in a reaction to l @66'>ic, .but
about 2 hours from the opening buying became
very good again, and hoisted to 66*Â»c. Early
cables from Liverpool were somewhat disap
pointing, but Northwest markets were strong
and Minneapolis and Duluth receipts continued
to dwindle. Clearances were 865,000 bushels,
large enough to be a bull factor. One decided
ly unfavorable feature was the inability of
shippers to procure cars from the railroads for
I new business. It interfered almost entirely
i with business from this point. After noon the
market became weak. The positive statement
was made that Russia was offering wheat more
freely and that Russian export stocks of un
sold wheat were much larger than expected.
This and the breaking of drought in California
brought large quantities of long wheat into
the market and sent prices down rapidly. Near
the end of the session Liverpool reported se
rious damage to the Argentine wheat crop,
but this had no effect in staying the liquida
tion, and the market was weak to the close:
May declined to 6o%!(2'6ciÂ»ic and closed at 65Â»ic.
Corn was rather dull. The market early
was helped by the wheat strength and higher
cables. As in wheat, the shortage of cars for
shipping business seriously interfered with
cash trading and this in turn depressed the
speculative market. The late weakness of
wheat also had un unfavorable effect, consid
erable corn bought early being put back on the
market. May closed >;c lower.
There was not enough trade in oats to make
an Independent market and prices followed
corn. May closed >Â»S^4c lower.
Provisions were active and quite irregular.
The market opened weak and lower, due la an
other heavy run of hogs, but good local and
outside support caused prices to score a good
advance. Packers were liberal sellers through
out and when the early demand had been sat
isfied the market lost all its strength and
prices slowly declined again. The close was
rather heavy. May pork, 2> c lower; May
lard, 2>^!(isc lower, and May ribs 2 : ii&.'.c lower

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Dec. 14.â The American Wool and
C tton Reporter will say to-morrow of the
w< 1 trade:
A large business has been transacted In wool
during the past week. This Is the result of
liberal purchasing by manufacturers, large and
small. There has also been some buying by
outside parties on speculative- account. The.
purchasing has been of a general character
and some good lines of domestic wools have
been taken. Including No. 1 washed . and un
washed fleeces, medium wools, spring Califor
nia and Texas wools, as well as good-sized
amounts of territory. Pulled wools have also
been conspicuous for their activity.
Prices show no improvement, however; in
fact, sales wore made last week as at low
figures as has been seen this year, and at no
time have fine fleeces sold at as low prices as
were paid for wools last week.
The sale of wools in Boston amount to
3.55,";.00n pounds on domestic and 3,336,000 pounds
foreign, making- a total of 5,219,000 pounds,
against a total of 3,037,000 pounds for the pre
vious week, and a total of 2,544, 400 pounds for
the corresponding week last year.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographle
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange. Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time bail on- the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error. If any. Is published
In the morning papers ,/ r>!^ >w ["K, da r v -; a
Vv. fe. rll'tjrlr.W,
Lieutenant. U. S. N.. in charge.
