â¢treat. Ban Francisco. -ISCHOOL BOARD
MAKES GOOD ITS
MANY PLEDGES
Eight "Subs" Given
Steady Jobs.
NEW CLASSES CREATED
JS.HS. BALL AND MISS BRADLEY
WILL BE REINSTATED.
Waller Scores Superintendent Web
ster and Gallagher Passes a
Compliment or so to Grand
Juryman Irving.
ft wag a warm time from roll call to
adjournment at yesterday's meeting of
the Board of Education. Nearly every
body called everybody else names, and the
fight for the last of the year jobs was
Jim a little bit more than spectacular.
Directors Waller, Gallagher and Head fig
ured rather more largely than any one
else. Gallagher didn't like Irving, Waller
didn't like Head and Head didn't like
any of them, and none spared any words
In Faying so.
Pledges were kept to the teachers
whose names have been for months on
the substitute list. Eight of them were
given schools yesterday and the last two
meetings has cleared the list for the ap
pointments of thirteen, more before the
unhappy last of the year arrives. Four
teachers were taken from the unassigned
Hat and assigned to places that were cre
ated for them by the formation of new
classes. Two were relegated to the un
assigned list for a reason tersely put by
Director Sam Waller, "that the unas
signed list was for the Incompetent, the
non compos mentis and those who were
too old to work." Exception was taken
to Mr. Waller's definition of the list, but
It was only Incidental to the bickerings
and horse play that characterized the re
malnder or the meeting. Wl*
The fun began with the reading of the
minutes. The records of every meeting
since some time in the early part of last
month were to be read, and Secretary
Welch skipped through half of them be
fore he asked for any possible exceptions.
He had got along as far as November
30. when Director Head took exception to
his system, and said that he had some
objections up his sleeve and he wanted
a change to put them in operation. It
transpired that his objections â¢ were on
the mutes Of the 30th. It was at that
meeting that Director Gallagher paid
his compliments in no chosen English to
Andrew Irving, chairman of the Grand
Jury committee on school affairs. Mr.
Gallagher had said that Irving had frac
tured his oath and jeopardized his in
tegrity by giving out to the newspapers
matters that should have been only for
the ears of the jury.! . Mr. Gallagher had
-aid these things, but not with that di
plomacy that is looked for in public
â records, and Mr. Head got up to ask that
all of the Gallagher report be stricken
from the records of the board.
"It was an unwarranted attack upon an
official of a dignified body." said Director
Head "who, I believe, was doing his duty. ,
It was through him that the present dis- i
graceful state of this board's finances was
bruught to our attention and to that of ;
*ur creditors. Mr. Gallagher's language 1
was, to say the least, undignifiedâ it was \
moreâ it- was a flow of billingsgate that
should never be allowed to appear upon
the records of this body. It was the per
sonal opinion of Gallagher himself, and
was of no interest to this board."
Derham seconded and Gallagher got up
to make no pointed defense. Dr. Ragan
tried his best to pour oil on the. disturbed
waters by a modifying amendment, but he
couldn't find a second, and the motion
went through as it was proposed.
A previous engagement kept Superin
tendent Webster elsewhere than at the
meeting, but he had a report in that did
as well in the way of raising another ruc
tion. Mr. Webster has lately been inves
tigating the condition of things at the
schools. Among those he has visited are
Bernal Heights, Garneld, Monroe, Nor
mal, Sherman and others. In his report
he said that at these places he found the
janitorial and scavenger service in need
of harsh criticism at least.
"That's his game, is it?" yelled Waller;
"clearing the boards so that the next
gang can appoint its own janitors on hiÂ«
recommendations. Why isn't he here to
night? He sends his report, but he is too
cowardly to come in here to back it up.
He's afraid to stand for what he has to
Bay, so he sneaks this in."
Gallagher took, the "buck" from Waller
and charged the absent Superintendent
with every infraction of the consolidation
act that he could conjure from his mem
ory, and the row waxed warm until it
was quieted by the politic President Bar
rington. '
However, Waller had been charged since
morning with a roast for some one, and
.somebody or other had to hear it. He
paid his respects to the teachers. He said
they were fools and had put their col
lective foot in their mouth by presuming
to sue for their plundered salaries. He
rsaid that all they could hope to get from
the courts was a decision that their sal
aries were not statutory, while the board
had always admitted that they were. "If
they had waited," said Mr. Waller, "they
â would have got their November pay, any
way. As it is, they are now liable to lose
their December salaries also." Director
Carew also thought that the teachers had

WHAT THEY ARE.
Composition of the Famous New
Food.
: A wide spread interest has been
created ai. ng good livers as to the
composition of Grape-Nuts, the new
food that has come into popular use of
late. It has long been known to physi-
cians, chemists and food experts that
the starchy portion of entire wheat
flour ana barley is transformed into a
true ariu very choice sugar, by the act
of the intestinal digestion in the human
body. This sugar is identical with,
and is known as grape-sugar, and it
is in condition for immediate transfor-
mation I to blood and the necessary
%â â¢ iructurc from which the delicate nerve
centers are built up.
â¢ A food expert of the Postum Cereal
Company, limited, Battle Creek, Mich.,
followed a line of experiments until he I
produce* the food called Grape-Nuts,
of which grape-sugar - forms the prin-
cipal part, and it is produced by follow- j
ing out nature's processes; in a me-
chanical way. Â. â  ...,'...
The n w food has a charming flavor
and can be served 'instantly- either cold
with cream, or by pouring hot milk or
cream over it, one has a hot mush at
once.
MOREHOUSE TO BE RETIRED
The Veteran of the Police Department Tires of
His Job.
POLICEMAN WILLIAM MOREHOUSE, the veteran of the depart
ment, yesterday sent in a written request to the commissioners to be
retired on a pension.
For years Morehouse has been a familiar figure at the crossing of
Sutter and Kearny streets. After being in the service for over twenty-four
years he has decided to lay aside the star and club to seek rest and com
fort In his old age.
Morehouse is now 75 years of ago. He was appointed a member of the
police department on October 9, 1574. Unable to do patrol duty on account
of his advanced years. Morehouse some years ago was assigned to the
crossing at Sutter and Kearny streets. Always gentle and courteous, he
soon gained the respect of the members of the fair sex, many of whom
will sincerely regret his retirement from the department.
It is said of him that he would purposely overlook a woman dressed in
silk to escort her poorly clad sister across the street.
The Pension Commissioners meet on the first of January, at which
timp Morehouse will be placed on the retired list.
Policeman Fred Smith has also asked the commissioners to retire him
on half pay. Smith was appointed a member of the force on April 24, IS7B,
and is 61 years of age. He has a host of friends, who will be pained to'
learn that he is about to sever his connection with the police department.
been hasty in thelfactlon and said that
he feared they had been led into a con
spiracy by which they would eventually
lose their vacation pay through a law
which it is now the scheme to get through
the next Legislature.
On top of all this Mr. Waller showed his
solicitude for the properly pilfered peda
gogue by introducing a resolution direct
ing Secretary Welch to prepare a state
ment of the school finances, which would
be mailed to each teacher. The resolution
passed.
Judge Belcher's recent decision reinstat
ing on the salary roll and in the good
graces of the Directors Mrs. Ball and
Miss Bradley, the teachers who were
summarily fired by the board, will be up
held. A resolution was Introduced yester
day taking them back into the depart
ment, but as there is a good bit of dis
cussion to come up it was held over until
next Friday, when the board will hold
another meeting.
J. J. Gallagher, who has been aaorning
the secondary substitute list, was by spe
cial resolution given a position as teacher
in the Mission High School.
The report of the elementary committee
was brought in by Waller and was adopt
ed. It made the following recomenda
tions:
That Miss M. O'Brien of the unassigned list
be transferred to a class In Sunnyside School;
a new class be formed In the Bay View Dis
trict, a part of Burnetts School, and Miss
Sophie Arguello, a substitute, be made teacher;

Grammar School to Lincoln Grammar School;

mar School; MUs B. M. Mlllhone. a substitute,

DEAN WILL BE RELEASED.
One of the Nevada Bank Forgers to
Be Given His Freedom.
It Is an open secret around the District
attorney's oince that A. H. Dean of Ne
vada Bank forgery fame will be released
from custody to-morrow. A motion to
that effect will be made in Judge Wal
lace's court and it 1b expected that It
will be granted without opposition.
Both the police and the Bankers' As
sociation are inclined to the belief that
Dean Is the least culpable of the gang.
The conviction of Becker, the arch-con-
Bpirator, waa brought about through him.
as the dangerous forger feared that
Dean's evidence might land him in the
penitentiary for life.
Dean was deserted by Becker shortly
after the commission of the forgery, and
as he refused to return him the amount
he had subscribed to the "defense fund"
of the trio, he readily accepted the propo
sition to turn State's evidence. Dean has
been In prison for over three years.

MILLIONS IN
GOLD FROM
AUSTRALASIA
To Balance Trade of
Three Countries.
HONOLULU'S NEW YORKERS
SOLDIERS CHEER THE ALA
MEDA'S STEWARD.
Presentation to Captain yon Oteren
dorp and Chief Officer Rennie
by the Balmain Regatta
Committee.
The mail steamer Alameda arrived from
Sydney, N. S. W., via Auckland, N. Z.,
Apia. Samoa, and Honolulu, H. I. From
the latter port she brought 320 soldiers
of the New York Regiment and the fol
lowing officers: Major Emmett, Surgeon
McCullough, Lieutenants Oliver, Gresham,
Tucker, Boyce, Oothoudt, Wood, Board
man and Captain Ferguson, Sergeant Ma
jor Wright and Charles W. Yeomans.
A number of sick men who were return-
Ing from Manila on the troopship Scandia
did not like the bill of fare on that ves
sel, so they paid their way up on the Ala
meda. They are: Day S. Hutchins,
hospital steward on the Scandia; Sydney
Callan, Thomas P. Davis, Pat Doolay, P.
O'Donnell, Jacob Evans, John H. Grabb,
Isaac Levy, Charles Morton, William J.
McCreedy, Charles Saunders, John Sloane
(from the gunboat Bennington), W. H.
Stroud (from the gunboat Bennington)
and Tom Elllnger. The Scandia was to
leave Honolulu last Saturday, and will
probably reach here next Sunday. She
will bring up the remainder of the New
Yorkers now on the islands.
If the food, served out to the men on the
Scandia was not all that could be de
sired, the soldiers on the Alameda must
have fared well. As soon as the cabin
passengers were landed and the boys
came ashore they sent a delegation
aboard again to bring Chief Steward O.
N. Clark on the wharf. As soon as lie
put in an appearance one of the officers,
on behalf of the volunteers, thanked him
for the royal manner in which they had
been cared for during the voyage, and
then they gave three cheers and a tiger
for Mr. Clark with such vigor that the
onlookers ceased to have any more sym
pathy for the invalids. Then every one
of the soldier boys had to shake the chief
steward by the hand and wish him good
by, so he was a pretty tired man when the
ceremony was over.
Among the cabin passengers by the Ala
meda were John D. Connelly, who for ten
years has been United States Consul at
Auckland, N. Z. ; William Sherer Jr. of
the Winchester Repeating Arms Com
pany and Professor W. M. Woodworth
and C. L. Crehore. The two latter gen
tlemen have been to Samoa in the inter
ests of Yale University. In those islands
there is a wormâ the Falolo wormâ that
lives and dies in a day. Only once during
the year is it seen. Then It puts in an
appearance at sunrise and at sunset dis
appears for another twelve months. Pro
fessor Woodworth and his companion
were successful in studying the life and
death of the worm, and are returning to
Yale well satisfied with the results of
their voyage.
The McKee-Rankin Company also re
turned on the Alameda. H. 3ch ussier
of the Spring Valley Water Company and
Mrs. ScnussTer returned from a visit to
the islands, and W. S. Burzacott, a mer
chant of Sydnoy, came up on a business
and pleasure trip. The Alameda brought
up among her cargo 120 boxes of sover
eigns and 24 boxes of bar gold valued at
J3.510 ,00u. This is one of the largest ship
ments of gold that has come here from
Australia in some time. It represents the
balance of trade between England, Aus
tralia and the United States. Thus San
Francisco sends many cargoes of wheat
to England and England very little mer
chandise here, so that the wheat has to
be paid for in cash. It would be very ex
pensive to send the gold to New York and
then over the continent, so as Autsralia
is always in debt to the mother country
for merchandise, the matter is so ar
ranged that the money the colonies owe
England is sent here to pay for the. wheat
shipped to Europe. In this way everybody
is paid and a month's interest on $3,510,
000 is saved.
A notable passenger by the Alameda
was "Cocker Tweedie, champion bantam
weight of Australia. It will be remem
bered that when Tweeaie was defeated by
Lawler about a year ago he disappeared.
He took his defeat so much to heart that
he got on the Alameda the next day and
returned to Sydney. Since then he has
developed into an actor-pugilist, and lett
the Bland Holt Company to return to San
Francisco to retrieve his laurels. He and
Third Assistant Engineer -.George Brown
of the Alameda are great friends, and
Brown resigned his position as soon as the
vessel docked in order to be with the
champion.
While lying in Sydney harbor the Ala
meda was the flagship at the Balmain re
gatta. The Sydney Isews said of her:
Certainly one of the attractions of the carni
val was the line steamship Alameda acting as
flagship. Despite the fact that the Alameila
was engaged up till midnight of Tuesday In
the grimy occupation of coaling, the steamer
presented an excellent appearance, looking
more as If it had been specially prepared for
a pleasant cruise than If it had been subjected
so recently to the dirty operations incidental
to taking In fuel. Aboard the Alameda was the
genial Commander Van Oterendorp. who. aided
by his officers, did all he could to render the
stay of his vistiors on board as enjoyable as It
could reasonably be made. It Is estimated that
there wore about 2000 visitors to the flagship.
Before the Alameda sailed for San
Francisco the regatta committee, through
the Mayor of Balmain, presented the
captain with a silver fruit dish and the
chief officer with a gold ring as a me
mento of the occasion. On the fruit dish
was engraved: "Balmain Regatta, Prince
of Wales' Birthday, November 9, 1898.
Presented by the Committee to Captain
K. Van Oterendorp, Flagship R. M. S.
Alameda." On Mr. KennFe's ring was in
scribed, "Presented by the Balmain Re
gatta Committee, November 9, 1898."
Both officers were entertained at a lunch
eon after the presentation.
The steamer Alice Blanchard arrived
from Clipperton Island yesterday. She
brought up the Japanese who went down
some months ago to work the guano de
posit, and 300 tons of guano.
Fire again broke out in the hold of tne
Shenandoah at noon yesterday. It seems,
to be burning about the stump of thd
mainmast. One stream of water keeps it
in control.

y *The schooner "W. F. Jewett has made

The Oceanic Steamship Company s Aus
tralia sailed for Honolulu yesterday. She

The ceremony attending the laying of
the corner stone of the new building of
the Mount Zion Hospital Association will
be held on next Sunday at 2 p. m. The
building: will occupy a lot on the south
side of Sutter street between Scott and
Devisadero and Is Intended to be a wel
come addition to the beautiful structures
of this city.

and are talking about reducing th-j speed. It

and struggle and compete in business affairs

the muscles, the whole system gives out. For
restoring strength after business worries.

run-down and the weak.
REAL ESTATE
STILL SLOWLY
ADVANCING
The Past Week Shows
Progress.
HINDERED BY THE WEATHER
MANY SALES OF ENCOURAGING
VALUE NEGOTIATED.
A Glance at the Records, Recent
Sales, New Building Contracts
and General Realty
Notes.
Real estate Is slowly progressing, not
withstanding the many obstacles thrown
in its way to retard its improving course
from almost absolute business stagnation
to a condition of healthy advancement.
Sales, although not of very encouraging
value, are still Increasing 1 in number
weekly and are maintaining a certain and
uniform value that brings encouragement
to the brokers of the city.
Building, considerng that it is now al
most the dead of winter, Is exceptionally
active. Contracts were let during the
week that exceeded in extent and value
almost the record of two of the past sum
mer months combined. One of the prin
cipal facts that the criterion of percepti
ble advancement is based on is that these
building operations are not confined to
any one section of the city, but are gen
erally scattered over and throughout the
entire city.
The only hindrance to advancement in
the general market seems to hinge on the
continuation of the adverse atmospheric
conditions that wrought havoc in the
"world of realty"' a year ago.
The record of mortgages and trust deeds
was unusually large for the past seven
days and involved an aggregate pledge of
nearly $200,000. The total value of re
leases and reconveyances was slightly in
advance of that for mortgages, amount
ing in all to $228,650.

and trust deeds recorded, amounting to a total
of $103,11*8. For the same period forty-three re

the total sum of $228,650.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
WILL MAKE
NO DEFENSE
FOR BRANDES
His Attorneys Will
Wait for Trial.
HE IS GROWING FAT IN JAIL
HIS WIFE IS BREAKING DOWN
IN HEALTH.
More Damaging Testimony That the
Rope Was Put Around Lillian's
Neck After Her
Death.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, 1 >cc. 14.
W. A. Brandes will have to itnswer in
the Superior <"<>urt to the charge of mur
g his daughter Lillian. His prelim
inary examination has not yet ended, but
/v\RS.W.A^BP>AMDE=S
Principals in the Sensational Murder Case Now Being Tried in Oakland.
his attorneys have already announced
that they will not i>ut in a defense at this
time.
Dr. J. M. Shannon, who was present at
the examination of the- murdered girl's
body, was <>r. the witness stand to-day.
He followed closely along the line of tes
timony given by Dr. Buteau, and declared
it as his opinion that Lillian died from
blows on the head and not from stran
gulation. Then Mr. Bennett asked:
"Is li not possible, doctor, that all the
evidences of hanging may be absent and
yet that death may have been caused by
hanging?"
"Tea, that is possible," was the reply.
'â¢Then, all the evidences being absent in
this case does not necessarily prove that
death was not caused by hanging?"
"That might be."
"Then that fact destroys your reason
for Baying that death was not caused by
hanging'"'
"It does in a measure."
Prosecuting Attorney Allen tried to
have the witness justify this statement,
which was in direct conflict with other
medical testimony given in the case, and
was partially HuocessfuL
Dr Eastman, who was the next wit
ness gave very damaging testimony. He
stated positively that Lillian died of
meningitis induced by blows on the head.
and h< ; the idea of death being
caused by hanging. .
Brandea passes his time during the long
scientific examination reading the news
papers He reads every line about the
case that he can procure, and frequently
requests newspaper men to bring him a
paper. Although the proceedings were
Somewhat dull to the lobby throughout
the day, there is always something crop
ping up to connect the leather club rest
a.L on the Judge's desk with the little
mound in the graveyard where lies the
13-year-old victim of the tragedy. It is
brought up when the prosecuting attor
ney will in the middle of a series of ques
tions calmly ask, "Would these condi
tions apply, doctor, to a girl 13 years old?"
The Leather club which Brandea carried
to use on burglars or highwaymen that
he might meet In the Berkeley wilds flg
ured very prominently several times to
day it was handed to the doctor, with
the question, "Would an instrument like
this have caused the wound?" and the
answer would invariably be that just such
an instrument would most certainly
cause wounds similar to those found on
tbe head of poor Lillian.
The examination will probably end to
morrow. Prosecuting Attorney Alien said
to-day that the State had nearly linished
its case, ami that it will conclude to-mor
row. Attorney Sawyer, who is associated
with the defense, said to-night that they
will not waste any time making a de
fense in the Justice's Court. "We are
confident that Brandea will be held to
answer," said the attorney "and we see
no use in covering the ground twice. We
have a good defense, but we do not think
it advisable to mako it at this time. In
the Superior Court we shall be prepared
to show the innocence of the accused."
Mrs. Brandes is still as uncommunica
tive as ever in the County Jail. She per
sistently declines to discuss her affairs or
those of her husband with newspaper
men, and gives to all the same answer,
"What is the use of paying an attorney
and not following his advice? He has or
dered me to say nothing."
Mrs. Brandes. however, has shown at
times an Inclination to defend her hus
band. She admits that she chastised
Lillian and has made the remark, "Al is
innocent," to Jailer White. It is not yet
certain what use will be made of the
woman as a witness, or whether she will
be called by the prosecution or the de
fense. The proof of the legality of her
marriage is still kept in the background
by both the prisoners, who decline to dis
cuss the matter. Should she preserve her
present frame of mind there is no doubt
that she will not turn State's evidence.
Her imprisonment tells heavily upon her,
while Brandes appears fresher and fatter
every time he is brought into court.

A Woman's Body Found Near a Box
of Rough on Rats.
OAKLAND, Dec. 14.â An unknown wo
man was found dead in a room at the
Avenue House, corner of Sixteenth street
I and San Pablo avenue, to-night. She is
: a stranger and was last seen by the land
lady on Monday last, when she asked
whether transient guests were taken. She
! secured a room, and when the landlady
! to-night went to her quarters to learn
whether s-he wished to keep the room
longer she found her lying on the bed,
] partly dressed and dead. On a small
I table near the head of the bed stood a
! box of "rough on rats, from which
I a good portion had been taken by the de
â  ceased. Nothing was found by which |
she might be identified.
The deceased was apparently about 3j
years of age. She is of medium build,
had a plain dark coat and dark skirt and j
black felt sailor hat. Her dark brown
):;iir is short and curly, and the left eye
, tooth is missing. Thu remains were taken
. to the Morgue.
There May Be a Widow.
OAKLAND, Dec. 14.â The remains of
O. A. Donohue. a veteran of the civil war,
I who died at the home in Yountville last
Monday, will be buried from this city
1 probably under the auspices of the G. A.
|K. The deceased lived here for many I
1 years and was widely known among the
oldi r residents. He was a native of
Massachusetts, aged 61 years, and in IS6I
and 1564 served under General Shafter.
Of late years he had been an intense suf
ferer from sickness, and on this account
w. Nt to the Soldiers' Home at Yount
ville. Had not his friends here heard of
his death in time the remains would have
been buried there. Some of these friends
: are interested in ascertaining: as to what ;
has become of Donohue's pension money,
! which amounted to $13 per month and
which, it Is claimed, has been collected
for a long time past by some woman in
San Francisco, who might claim now that
she is Donohue's widow.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, DECEMBER 15, 1898.
BABY SELLING
INDULGED IN
BY SOCIETIES
Sharp Says Daniels
Asks for Coin.
HENDERSON IS DEFENDED
IS A MINISTER IN GOOD STAND
ING.
Home-Finding for Children in Cali
fornia Seems to Be a Fit
Subject for Investi
gation.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, .uec. 14.
The complaint made by the Society for
the Prevention of Cruelty to Children re
garding Rev. J. E. Henderson of the
American Home Finding Association of
W.A.BRANDES
the Pacific Coast, with its headquarters
in this city, has brought to light some of
the methods by which children are traf
ficked in throughout California.
The complaint made against 'JLc. Hen
i derson was published in this morning's
; Call and attracted wide attention in this
! city on account of the prominent people
i interested in and associated with the Oa^
! land society.
Jion. Cralgie Sharp, presfdent of the Oak
! land society, says that the charges maue
I in to-day's Call by Rev. F. Daniels of Sac
i ramento are largely unfounded, and he
j replies to them by making just as seri
; ous charges against Mr. Daniels.
There are several societies in Callfor
' nia. It apears, which make it a business
of findiiig children and finding homes for
them. They have solicitors going all over
the State collecting large sums of money
which are mostly spent for the salaries
of those running the society.
The Oakland society has for its board
of directors many of the leading minis
ters and merchants in this city, and, ac
cording to Its president, is doing work
that saves the State thousands of dollars
per month. It is managed by Rev. J. j E.
Henderson, who is assisted by three ladies
who solicit subscriptions all over the
State for the society and are allowed 50
per cent commission for their salary and
expenses.
"I have perfect confidence in Mr. Hen
derson." said President Sharp, "but I
know that Rev. Mr. Daniels has frequent
ly made a demand for money on people
with whom he has placed children. It is
this system that has led to the report that
home-finding societies sell children com
ing into their possession. I have here
proof from Petal uma that Mr. Daniels
placed a child with a familyldemanded $50.
and when it was not forthcoming the child
was turned over to us and we found it a
home. There are several such instances
which we can prove. Our society does
not do this, and I believe Mr. Henderson
is acting honestly and usefully. He is ndt
a disgraced minister, but is a member in
good standing of the Methodist Confer
ence. Regarding anything he may have
done at Angels Camp I know nothing.
"The two children which it is said we
took from Sacramento I know nothing
about, but I have no doubt that Rev. J. E.
Henderson has placed them in a tempo
rary home, and if they are wanted at Sac
ramento they can be sent there at once.
We have no home in which to place chil
dren, and the expense consists in taking
the children from one city to another. It
is true Mr. Henderson travels free and
can take his wards with him, but he has
to meet his hotel expenses, and although
his salary is supposed to be $7o a month
I know that he has not yet been able to
get that much from the society. This
matter in the paper is the result of jeal-
ousy.
"Orlgrlnally we were a portion of an
; other society, but they wanted too gTeat
! a percentage of the collections made in
1 this city, and we decided to organize inde
i pendently. We are now about to incorpo
1 rate, and our board of directors includes
â  many of the best men in Alameda.
County." . ..
Rev. J. E. Henderson is now in the north
ern part of the State, and Is expected back
to-morrow. President Sharp says he will
no doubt lay the statements of Rev. Mr.
Daniels before the attorney of the society
and will probably demand satisfaction.
M J. Keller, who is the treasurer of
the society, says he does not know any
thing about Mr. Henderson,- but he has
absolute confidence in President Sharp,
and knows that he would not be a party
to anything that was not perfectly
straightforward and worthy of support.
ALAMEDA DEMURS TO
A BIG DAMAGE SUIT
LITIGATION OVER THE DEATH
OF MRS. DAVOUST.
Question as to Who Was Responsible
for the Wire That Burned
Her to Death.
OAKLAND, Dec. 14.â The city of Ala
meda, through its attorney, Senator B.
K. Taylor, filed a demurrer this after
noon to the complaint of Martial Davoust
in his suit against the city, the Sunset
Telephone and Telegraph Company and
Pacific Postal Telegraph Company,
brought recently for the recovery of $50,
000 damages on account of the death of
his young wife, which was caused by a
live wire.
The demurrer is very elaborate. Its
iirst ground is the insufficiency of facts
to constitute a cause. Another point is
that the complaint is uncertain in that it
cannot be ascertained therefrom which
of the defendants Btretched or maintained
the "certain wire," or whether said wire
was a telegraph, telephone or electric
light wire; also that it does not specify
which, if any, of the defendants â¢'negli
gently and carelessly allowed" the wire to
remain upon the ground, or who devel
oped or conducted the electricity that
heavily charged said wire.
The fact that the complaint does not
specify what purtion of the alleged dam
ages was the result of the loss of ser
vices and society of plaintiff's wife, and
what portion is claimed for the bodily and
mental Injuries sustained by plaintiff is
another point urged in the demurrer. Also
that two causes of action have been im
properly joined. And finally that there Is
a misjoinder of parties defendant, in that
it appears from the complaint that each
defendant was operating a separate sys
tem of wiring, supported by the same
poles, that "a certain wire" caused the
injury, and It is apparent that as any one
wire was maintained by one defendant
only there is no cause for the joinder of
the other two defendants.
The city asks to be dismissed as a de
fendant.
ONLY THIRTY DAYS.
Short Sentence of F. B. Willey, a Con-
victed Burglar.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Dec. 14.
F. B. "Willey, a burglar of Kings county,
was brought to the county jail to-night to
be kept until he shall be taken to San
Quentln. Willey considers himself one of
the luckiest men in California. He has
to serve a sentence of thirty days, the
smallest sentence ever passed In Cali
fornia upon a conviction of burglary.
Willey and a companion named Jose
Estuardo were caught while engaged in
a burglary. When arraigned Estuardo
pleaded guilty and was sentenced to San
Quentin for five years. Willey pleaded
not guilty and put the State to the ex
pense of a trial, after which he was con
victed and, to the surprise of everybody,
he was sentenced to thirty days in San
Quentin.
The deputy sheriff who is bringing Wil
ley to San Quentin says that the whole of
Kings county is Incensed at the action of
Judge Dixon Phillips, who sentenced Wil
ley. He says it was confidently expected
that Estuardo's sentence would be mitt
gated In view of the fact that he pleaded
guilty, instead of which he received the
maximum penalty for burglary in the
second degree. The prosecution was con
fident that with the conviction of Willey
he would receive the same sentence as
his pal, but to the surprise of the Dis
trict Attorney and the whole of the coun
ty, he was let off with thirty days.
"It is not worth the expense of bring
ing him to the State's prison," said the
deputy, and Willey Jocularly says that
the trip is an agreeable excursion for
him.
Halpin's Death.
OAKLAND. Dec. 14.â The jury at the
inquest of Dennis Halpln, the aged gar
dener who was run down by reckless driv
ers last Sunday night on Moss avenue,
brought in a verdict to-night that "death
was caused by the fracture of several
ribs and vertebrae, the result of being
thrown from his wagon through some un
known cause."

' ALAMEDA. Dec. 14.â The charges pre

Albert Kamp will be investigated Friday
night by the police committee of the

High School Student Officers.
BERKELEY, Dec. 14.â The senior class
of the Berkeley High School held a meet
ing yesterday afternoon for the purpose
of electing officers. The following were
chosen: President. Arthur Lowell; vice
president, Miss Carrie Johnson; secre
tary. Miss C. Parvln; treasurer, Miss
Pearl Wagner; sergeant at arms. Howard
Hendricks; class prophet. Miss Kate
Peake; class historian, Miss Leslie Trow
bridge. A motion providing that no play
be given the class day exercises before
graduation was lost, and steps will be
taken to choose a cast by the beginning
of next term.

WALTER ROSSER'S TRIAL.
The Slayer of Henry Hildebrand Does
Not Remember the Tragedy.
The trial of Walter Rosser, the youth
ful soldier who killed Henry Hildebrand
on September 13, will probably conclude
to-day. When the case was again taken
up yesterday morning Rosser was called
to the witness chair. He told in substance
the same story as upon the former trial.
He said that after he left the Pine street
dive he remembered nothing of his actions
and of the tragedy he had not the slight
est recollection. He did not recover from
the effects (if some poison he believed was
administered to him until the next day.
After the prisoner left the stand sewrai
witnesses corroborated his story, and then
witnesses were called by the prosecution
in rebuttal.

THE RAIN CAME
JUST IN TIME
The Hearts of the Farmer, the Stock
man and the Storekeeper Are
Made Glad.
WEATHER FORECASTER HAMMON when seen yesterday said
that the rain which began to fall on Tuesday night has been
doing its duly. It has covered the entire State of California,
from Point Conception and north of Tehachapi, where upward
of half an inch has fallen.
"I am afraid," said Mr. Hammon, "that it will soon stop, al
though the storm may return to us with redoubled vigor. It has
rained nicely and evenly over the largest portion of the State. The
Sacramento Valley has had a little less than an inch, about three
quarters fell at the lower end of the San Joaquin and Stockton and
half an inch at San Luis Obispo. There has been a trace of rain at
Los Angeles, and the storm, which had Its source in the west of Ore
gon, is slowly moving to the southward.
"The rain has been of considerable benefit, especially to
stock. The shower has averaged about half an inch. It will start
the grass growing and this will come at what I might call a critical
point. When the last frost made its appearance it is a fact that the
stock all over the State did not have the strength to withstand it, and
at least 10 per cent perished. A stock raiser in Monterey County,
whose cattle were suffering, contracted for twenty cars to ship
them to Nevada, but the present rain may have the effect of keeping
his stock here.
"The wheat growers are also jubilant, but before good crops can
be insured there must be considerably more rain. I consider the
present visitation only as a 'good sample.' There is no conclusive
indexations that we will have more rain in the next twenty-four hours,
but if the storm off Oregon is strong enough it will probably send us an
other secondary storm like the first and we may expect rain again
on Friday morning. The only bad feature that I can see now is that
the wind is northeast, but if it veers around to the south, and there
Is no reason why it should not, we will have abundant rains. It is
clear at Red Bluff and Eureka to-night and the storm is drawing off
the coast to the west. Thursday night or Friday morning will most
probably see a return of the welcome visitation."
From all parts of the State come jubilant reports and the farm
ers, orchardists and vineyardists are happy and hopeful, while the
stockmen are open in expressing iheir intense satisfaction. San
Jose, San Diego, Sacramento, Fresno and Los Angeles have been
cheered by the downpour. The fall in Los Angeles has been com
paratively light, but .10 of an inch, although it was still raining
there last night. The local health authorities are also delighted,
for the rain has flushed many blocked sewers and has cleared the
atmosphere of the irritating particles which are held responsible for
the throat, nasal and pulmonary troubles with which Callfornians, as
a rule, are afflicted. Even the merchants are glad, for the prospect
for a dry winter made the outlook for holiday sales very black.
Purse strings will now be loosened and the interior merchants espe
cially will reap the benefits of the welcome rains. This means good
business all around and California's prosperity for the coming year is
assured.

Turned on the Gas.
Owen O'Neill, a rancher from Chlco,
had a narrow escape from death in his
room in Hackmeier's Hotel. Eddy street.
When he retired Tuesday night he turned
off the gas. but accidentally turned it on
again. He was discovered yesterday
morning in an unconscious condition, and
i Drs. Calvin and Baldwin were callpd
in. The ambulance was also sum
moned, but It was decided not to remove
him. He will probably recover.

FAIR NATIVES
WILL HAVE A
NEW HOME
Pleasant Place Where
They Can Go.
DEDICATION NEXT MONTH
IT IS A SMALL BEGINNING,
BUT IT WILL GROW.
Hopes and Plans of the Order
Explained by One of
the Prominent
Officers.
For many months a number of the
members of the order of Native Daugh
ters of the Golden West have worked on
the lines of establishing a home for the
members of the organization in which
such as desire may. when away from
their own homes, have a place to go
where thoy will find "all the comforts of
home" in the fullest sense of the phrase.
In the carrying out of this Idea the
Board of Relief of the Grand Parlor ap
pointed a committee of five, said com
mittee to have full power to plan for the
project in the manner they deemed best, of
which the following named ladies are the
officers and directors: Mrs. Tiny O'Kane,
the senior past grand president, who by
reason of the fact that she was the first
president of the Grand Parlor was given
the post of honor, that of president; Miss
Mac B. "Wilkins, past grand president,
vice president; Miss Clara K. Witten
nieyer, past grand president, secretary;
and Mrs. Carrie R. Durham, past grand
president, treasurer. Mrs. W. S. Leake
of Califa Parlor, Mrs. 11. M. Green of
Buena Vista Parlor, Mrs. J. A. Stein^ach
of Alta Parlor, Miss Minnie Coulter of
Santa Rosa Parlor and Miss Mollie B.
Johnson of Califa Panor, directors.
That step having been taken, the
ladles named occupied themselves In the
matter of Interesting the parlors, not
dnly of this city but of the entire State,
and by personal visits to such parlors as
they could reach, and by correspondence,
they laid their proposition before the
membership. Their labors were rewarded
by assurance of assistance. Buena Vista
Parlor voted $50 from its treasury to aid
the cause along, and a day or \wo after
Alta Parlor contributed $100, the Oro Fino
Parlor gave $50, the parlor at Buena \ en
tura gave $15 and Marguerite Parlor do
nated $10 and Forrest $20. Subsequently
Buena Vista added to its donation in the
shape of an offer to Tit up one room in a
building to be selected as a hospita .
Then there came offers of assistance
from a great number of the parlors, con
ditional to this extent: "If you have your
home in operation by the Ist of January,
lsy.t and there is a prospect that it will be
a success, then we will assist you in your
project " This was so encouraging that
the energetic officers held a meeting on
the 10th of September and appointed Mes
dames Leake, Green and Steinbach and
Misses Wittenmeyer and Wilkins a com
mittee to secure suitable quarters in
which to establish the home. After a
protracted search, house hunting on a
grand scale, the committee decided to take
a ten-room house at 925 O'Farrell street,
between Polk street and Van Ness avenue,
a pleasant, quiet neighborhood. This was
not however, until the latter part of Oc
tober. Then came a difficult task, the
renovation of the premises. The landlord
kindly offered to put It in good order, and
now the house is a very neat and comfort
able one, and while it is not very large,
it is sufficient for the time being.
After the painters, the kalsominers and
other artisans had vacated, Mrs. D. T.
Wilkins the mother of the vice president,
was appointed matron with instruction to
take charge of the place and see that it
was put in proper condition for the re
ception of guests. There came in the
first place, in the nature of a most pleas
ant surprise, a donation by Mrs. Leake of
carpets and furnitureâ almost enough to
furnish five rooms. This lady has taken
an unusual interest in this matter and
has done and is still doing much to makes
the home the grand success that it is, iv
time, bound to be. Buena Vista Parlor
also gave furnishing for a hall bedroom
on the upper floor, with the understand
ing that Jn case of need it shall be used
for hospital purposes. The other room
which is to be the hospital proper will
be furnished by that parlor in a few days.
Mrs. Wilkins added to the contribution of
furniture and at this time the ten rooms
are very neatly and comfortably fur
nished. There is on the half basement
floor a dining room, kitchen, pantry and
servants' room. On the first floor there
is a pretty, well-lighted double parlor and
matron's "room, while on the upper floor
are four bedrooms, one of which will be
the hospital, and a bathroom. There are,
of course, a number of things necessary
to adnrn the walls, in the line of pictures
and fancy articles that go to make up the
furnishing of a house complete, but there
is hope within the breasts of the directors
and officers that a number of liberally dis
posed Native Daughters and Native Sons
will in the fullness of their hearts send
a number of such articles as Christmas
gifts to the home. The mother of the sec
retary contributed 100 glasses of jelly for
the dining room pantry.
"What we propose to do," said Miss
Wilkins, the vice president, "is to give
notice to all members of the order that
any member of a parlor in good stand
ing who desires may come to the home
and there find such accommodations as
they can find in their own home and that
at a small cost. Such will be able to ar
range with the matron, if so desired, to
take meals at the home or take them ;
wherever desired. Take, for instance, i
members of the order who come to this !
city alone and do not wish to be a bur
then on friends or acquaintainces or do
not desire to go to a hotel or lodging
house; such can come to the home and .
there will have all the privileges they i
would enjoy in their own home, i
The parlors will be at their dis
posal, and occasionally there will
be home gatherings for social entertain
ment. We feel much gratified at the en
couragement we have received and we
hope that the time is not far distant
when we shall have to seek larger quar
ters and we look forward to the time
when we can own our own home and \
have the pleasure of seeing the Native
Daughters' Home one of the most promi
nent institutions of the State. We expect
that we will receive contributions of ;
books in order to establish a library for
the use of those who shall stop with us,
be it for a short or a long period."
It is hoped that some of the wealthy
people of the city and State who are na
tives thereof but not members of the or
der will, when they understand the new
institution, contribute to its support.
The home will be dedicated on the 2d
of next January.
TO WALK SIX DAYS
FOR CHARITY'S SAKE
BIG CONTEST TO START IN THE
PAVILION TO-DAY.
The attendance at the charity bazaar
has been gradually but steadily increas
ing, and hast night, in spite of the threat
ening weather, the attendance was as
laige as any night since the opening.
The great six-day walking contest in
the gallery of the Pavilion will start at
1:30 o'clock this afternoon. There were
fifty entries, but C. W. Doyle, who is
booking special attractions, cut them
down to twenty, selecting only the best
men. A five-foot tan bark track has been
laid around the gallery, and refreshment
booths will be put at each end for the
benefit of the contestants. The track is
seven laps to the mile, and each contest
ant will be given the privilege of an
hour's rest any time he sees fit to drop
out. The race opens at 1:30 o'clock each
day and continues until 11:30, a total of
ten hours in twenty-four.
Music is now being furnished by bands
from the various Native Sons' parlors,
which have entered into the musical con
test.
RATES APPROVED.
Railroad commissioners Accept the
Valley Road's Vegetable Tariff.
The Railroad Commissioners held their
monthly meeting yesterday. The most
important thing done by the board was to
adopt the special fresh fruit and fresh
vegetables tariff No. 1 of the San Fran
cisco and San Joaquin Valley Railroad,
which was submitted by that road. This
new schedule of rates Is lower than that
for grain given by the Southern Pacific.
It is for all points between Bakersfleld
and San Francisco, and gives a rate of
20 cents on the hundred. Similar rates are
also given between Bakersfield and
Stockton.
The consideration of the right of the
board to investigate the affairs of the
Pullman Palace Car Company went over
until the next meeting, the board deeming
it best to await the advice of the Attor
ney General, who has the matter under
consideration.
The report of I. H. Polk, expert to the
board, who, pursuant to a resolution
passed by the board on April 22, has been
investigating the subject of the regulation
of the rates of charges for transportation
of freight and passengers on lines in its
â¢jurisdiction, was read and filed. The
Southern, Central and South Pacific Rail
way companies were the ones dealt with
in the report by Mr. Polk. After review
ing the case, which hns been pendlnp for
a long time before the board, Mr. Polk
says in conclusion:
â¢'I wish to say that in this railroad case
we have gradually accumulated and are
accumulating a mass of facts and figures
which, when properly analyzed and tabu
lated, will, in my opinion, prove of Incal
culable value not only to your honorable
board in the pending and future inquiries
into the methods of this great labyrinth
of corporations but also to all other
boards and officers of this State whose
duties bring them into contact with rail
road companies."
CAPTAIN DE LA MAR IS HERE.
Made Famous by the Divorce Granted
Him From His Wife.
Captain J. R. de La Mar and T. M.
Clement are registered at the Palace. The
former is tho owner of many profitable
mines and is well known here, while his
companion is also a man of mining fame.
They are en route to Mexico to look after
some of their mining interests, and are
accompanied by Guy Anderson, an assay
er of Salt Lake.
Captain de La Mar is prominent the
world over not only on account of his
wealth but because, of the recent divorce
proceedings that he instituted in Paris
against his wife. She was a poor girl of
Parkersburg, W. Va., when he married
her and was said to have been, in the
language of the Parisian critics, a god
dess of divine beauty and grace. He took
her to Europe, where her beauty and
form attracted much attention. They vis
ited Egypt, and it is said that Mrs. de
La Mar was the recipient of marked at
tention from the Khedive.
This was the beginning of the end, and
Captain de La Mar instituted divorce pro
ceedings. It is said that he found in his
wife's possession letters from the Khe
dive of a compromising nature. He was
granted a divorce. This was five years
ago, and those who know the captain say
that he will never again take unto him
self a wife.
Mr. Clement, who is the general man
ager of Captain de La Mar's mines, be
came famous throuph his connection with
John Hays Hammond, with whom he
was associated in the Jamison raid in
South Africa. He was captured by the
forces of Oom Paul and only escaped
with his life through the interference of
the British Government.
